# Helios - Interview Guide

**Autonomous Growth Diagnosis Engine - the definitive prep**

This guide is the single source of truth for defending Helios in placement interviews for Product, Growth, Data, Business, Analytics-Engineering, and AI/Data-Engineering roles. It pairs the concepts an interviewer probes with the exact Helios decision that proves you have done the work - so every answer moves from a general principle to concrete, quantified evidence.

---

## How to use this guide

**Study order.** Read the Fact Sheet below until you can reproduce it on a whiteboard from memory - the decomposition identity, the eval numbers, and the three pillars are non-negotiable. Then work the sections in order: the early sections build the problem framing and architecture mental model; the middle sections drill the technical centerpiece (mix-shift vs rate-change), the statistics, and the trust story; the later sections cover honesty/limitations, role positioning, and rapid-fire rebuttals. Skim everything once end-to-end before deep-drilling, so you can cross-reference.

**How to drill the Q&A.** Each Q&A is built as: (a) the concept stated generally, (b) the tradeoff and the failure mode it avoids, (c) the "Why Helios demonstrates it" tie-back to a specific named component. Do not memorize answers verbatim - internalize the *structure* so you can recombine pieces when a question comes in a shape you did not rehearse. For every question, force yourself to name a number, name an alternative you rejected, and name the component.

**The rehearse-out-loud rule.** Reading is not rehearsing. Say every answer aloud, end to end, under 90 seconds, before you consider it learned. If you cannot say it without filler or without inventing a number, you do not know it yet. Record yourself once per section; an answer you cannot deliver fluently out loud will collapse under follow-up pressure.

## The Helios Fact Sheet (memorize this)

**One-line thesis.** Helios is an always-on AI Growth Analyst that diagnoses *why* an e-commerce funnel moved, quantifies revenue-at-risk in dollars, and ships a prioritized, statistically-defensible experiment backlog - all grounded in governed SQL it never hand-authors.

**The problem in one line.** Dashboards report *what* happened, not *why*; manual root-cause takes ~1-3 analyst-days per anomaly, routinely confuses mix-shift with rate-change, and rarely carries dollar impact or a prescribed experiment.

**The architecture in 5 lines:**

| Layer | What it is |
|---|---|
| 5 MCP servers | warehouse-mcp (sole BigQuery client; dry_run -> run_query byte gate; reconcile <=0.5%), semantic-mcp (ONLY SQL path; build_query from governed registry), stats-mcp (ONLY math path), experiment-mcp (power/runtime/design), report-mcp (render_brief + memory) |
| 7 agents | Orchestrator (Opus), Monitor (Sonnet), Decompose (Sonnet), Diagnose (Opus), Critic (Opus), Prescribe (Sonnet), Narrator (Sonnet) - on a deterministic FSM; LLM composes tool calls, never controls flow |
| Semantic layer | semantic_layer.yaml: 47 metrics + 19 dimensions, referential-integrity compiled, maps 1:1 to dbt MetricFlow - single source of truth for all SQL |
| Memory | BigQuery helios_memory + vector store: diagnosis history (embeddings), suppression list, seasonality/launch calendars, action-tracking loop |
| Evaluation | Offline labeled benchmark: synthetic anomalies injected into a frozen GA4 copy; the pipeline must rediscover them |

**The decomposition identity (write it cold):**

> deltaR = **mix** + **rate** + **interaction**
> where R = sum_i (w_i * r_i),  mix = sum_i (dw_i * r_i(t0)),  rate = sum_i (w_i(t0) * dr_i),  interaction = sum_i (dw_i * dr_i)
> (w = segment volume share, r = segment rate). Computed deterministically by stats-mcp.decompose_change. Drill rate-effects (real behavior) before mix-effects (composition artifacts) - this dissolves Simpson's paradox.

**Numbers to never forget:**

| Metric | Value |
|---|---|
| Root-cause segment accuracy | **>=85%** vs **<=45%** naive baseline |
| Hallucinated columns | **0** |
| Decomposition MAPE | **<=10%** |
| Runtime | **<5 min/run** |
| BigQuery budget | **5 GiB** fixed |
| Labeled scenarios | **50** across 7 buckets |
| Semantic registry | **28 metrics / 16 dimensions** |
| Reconciliation tolerance | **<=0.5%** |

**The three defensibility pillars:** (1) **governed-SQL grounding** - semantic-mcp is the only SQL path (0 hallucinated columns); (2) **deterministic statistics** - stats-mcp is the only math path; (3) **adversarial verification + offline eval** - the Critic plus the 85%-vs-45% benchmark. Thesis: the hard part is not generating insights, it is making them *correct and trusted*.

**The four honesty points:** (1) dataset is **observational & historical** (Nov 2020-Jan 2021), so Helios *designs and sizes* experiments and runs quasi-experimental readbacks (pre/post, diff-in-diff) - it does not run live A/Bs; (2) **~3-month window** -> true LTV deferred, report short-horizon proxies with stated assumptions; (3) **user_id almost always null** -> identity is cookie-grain (user_pseudo_id), user-level metrics are approximations; (4) **traffic_source gotcha** -> event-level source is user FIRST-TOUCH not session source, so Helios derives session-scoped source/medium and falls back to first-touch.

**The canonical funnel:** session_start -> view_item -> add_to_cart -> begin_checkout -> add_shipping_info -> add_payment_info -> purchase. Revenue = purchase_revenue_in_usd. Session = (user_pseudo_id, ga_session_id). Dataset = bigquery-public-data.ga4_obfuscated_sample_ecommerce.

**The 10 GA4 channel groups:** Direct, Organic Search, Paid Search, Display, Paid Social, Organic Social, Email, Affiliates, Referral, Other.

---

## One-sentence positioning per role

- **Product Analyst:** "I built a system that goes past *what* a funnel did to *why* it moved - decomposing each conversion-step drop into mix vs rate, attaching dollar revenue-at-risk, and prescribing the experiment to fix it."
- **Growth Analyst:** "Helios turns an anomaly into a prioritized, power-sized experiment backlog with quantified upside, so growth bets are ranked by defensible impact rather than by whoever argues loudest."
- **Data Analyst:** "I automated the 1-3-day root-cause grind into a <5-minute run that never hallucinates a column, because every query is composed from a governed 28-metric semantic registry and reconciled to <=0.5%."
- **Analytics Engineer:** "I built a GA4 -> dbt warehouse - staging, sessionized intermediate, monotonic funnel flags, marts - with a semantic layer mapping 1:1 to MetricFlow as the single source of truth for every downstream metric."
- **AI/Data Engineer:** "I orchestrated 7 Claude agents on a deterministic finite-state machine over 5 MCP servers, where the LLM only composes governed tool calls and never controls flow or authors SQL - LLM judgment, deterministic guarantees."
- **Founder/Consulting:** "I shipped an autonomous analyst that closes the insight-to-action gap end-to-end - diagnosis, dollar impact, prescription, and an executive Decision Brief - and proved it on a 50-scenario labeled benchmark, not a demo."

---

## Answer voice reminder

Three rules govern every answer in this guide - obey them out loud, not just on paper:

1. **Concept first, then Helios.** Explain the general principle and why it matters *before* citing Helios. Never talk only about the project - show you understand the idea independently, then use Helios as the concrete proof.
2. **Quantify + tradeoff + honesty.** State a number, name the alternative you rejected and the tradeoff, and surface the relevant limitation. Honesty about constraints (observational data, cookie-grain identity, 3-month window) reads as senior judgment, not weakness.
3. **Tie to a specific component.** Close with a "Why Helios demonstrates it" line that names the exact server, agent, or artifact - semantic-mcp, the Critic, decompose_change, semantic_layer.yaml - using canonical names accurately. Tight, high-signal prose; no buzzword soup.

# Helios â€” Story & Elevator Pitches

## Section 1: Project Story

The same project, told five ways. Each version is tuned to one audience's attention budget and the one thing that audience is actually deciding. Use the version that matches the room; do not blend them.

### Resume (1-2 lines)

**Helios â€” Autonomous AI Growth Analyst (BigQuery Â· dbt Â· Claude Agent SDK Â· MCP).** Built a self-running, seven-agent diagnosis engine that distinguishes mix-shift from rate-change, prices funnel movement as dollars of revenue-at-risk, and prescribes a power-analyzed experiment backlog from governed SQL â€” **85%+ root-cause accuracy vs a 45% naive baseline**, graded automatically in CI, with **0 hallucinated columns**.

### Recruiter screen (~30 seconds, plain language)

"Helios is an AI system I built that acts like an always-on growth analyst for an online store. When a sales funnel moves â€” say checkout conversion drops â€” a human analyst normally spends one to three days figuring out *why*. Helios does it in under five minutes, on a schedule, without anyone asking. It figures out the real reason behind a number changing, puts a dollar value on it, and recommends the one experiment most worth running. I built it on Google's public e-commerce analytics dataset using BigQuery, dbt, and Claude's agent framework. The headline result: it gets the root cause right 85% of the time versus 45% for the obvious naive approach, and I prove that with an automated test suite that runs on every code change."

### Technical interview (~2 minutes, architecture + trust)

"Helios is an autonomous growth-diagnosis engine. The product loop is: detect an anomaly in a funnel metric, decompose *why* it moved, diagnose the root cause, verify it adversarially, size a fix, and ship an executive Decision Brief â€” all on a schedule.

The technical centerpiece is a **mix-shift versus rate-change decomposition**. Any aggregate rate `R = Î£ wáµ¢Â·ráµ¢` over segments, where `w` is each segment's volume share and `r` its rate. The change `Î”R` splits exactly into `mix = Î£ Î”wáµ¢Â·ráµ¢(t0)`, `rate = Î£ wáµ¢(t0)Â·Î”ráµ¢`, and an interaction term. That tells you whether conversion fell because in-segment behavior got worse, or merely because traffic composition shifted toward lower-converting segments. It's how I dissolve Simpson's paradox â€” the aggregate can fall while every segment rises.

The trust story is three structural pillars. First, **governed-SQL grounding**: the LLM never authors raw SQL. It composes governed metrics through a semantic layer â€” `semantic-mcp` is the *only* path to SQL â€” so hallucinated columns are structurally impossible, not just discouraged. Second, **deterministic statistics**: all decomposition, significance testing, power, and forecasting run in real Python via `stats-mcp`; nothing statistical is generated in token-space. Third, **adversarial verification plus offline eval**: a Critic agent tries to refute every finding â€” mix confound, insufficient sample, seasonality, data-quality artifact â€” and a 50-scenario labeled benchmark grades root-cause accuracy in CI on every commit.

Architecturally that's five MCP servers as a capability/trust boundary and seven agents on a deterministic finite-state machine. The LLM composes tool calls; it never controls flow."

### Hiring manager round (~2 minutes, impact + collaboration + decisions)

"The problem Helios attacks is the insight-to-action gap. Every growth org has dashboards that tell you *what* happened, but the expensive question is *why*, and that still routes to a human analyst for one to three days per anomaly. That analyst is a serial, manually-triggered bottleneck â€” and they routinely confuse a traffic-mix change with a real behavior change, which funds the wrong fix.

Helios inverts that. Diagnosis becomes continuous and proactive: every scheduled run re-derives the funnel, flags what moved, separates mix from rate, prices it in revenue-at-risk, and ships a brief the head of growth can forward to the CEO â€” *before* anyone asks. I designed it around four real personas: the Head of Growth who owns the revenue number, the PM who owns a funnel surface, the analyst who's tired of being the human RCA oracle, and the founder who just wants the three things that moved revenue and the plan.

The decision I'm proudest of is making trust *measurable* instead of asserted. 'The AI says so' isn't shippable, so I built a labeled benchmark and wired it into GitHub Actions: every commit grades diagnosis accuracy, and a regression that drops top-1 accuracy by more than two points or introduces any hallucination fails CI. The hardest tradeoff was scope discipline â€” it would have been easy to drift into a dashboard or a chatbot. I held a hard anti-product line: conversation is a secondary drill-down surface; the heartbeat is the autonomous run."

### Founder round (~2 minutes, business + ROI + what next)

"Helios creates a category rather than competing in one. Descriptive BI tells you *what*; conversational text-to-SQL tools answer one human-prompted question at a time and frequently hallucinate; product analytics gives you funnels with some *who*. None of them autonomously answer *why*, *how much in dollars*, and *what to do next*. That white space â€” autonomous, governed, statistically-defensible, dollar-quantified diagnosis â€” is the wedge.

The ROI math is direct. Manual root-cause analysis costs one to three analyst-days per anomaly, and a senior analyst's funnel is dozens of metrics across many segments every week. Helios takes that to under five minutes per run on a fixed BigQuery byte budget â€” and, more importantly, it stops misdiagnosis. Every week a depressed conversion rate goes undiagnosed is compounding lost revenue; every wrong mix-vs-rate call funds the wrong engineering work. Helios converts that invisible liability into a visible, prioritized, dollar-denominated backlog.

The wedge is deliberately narrow so it can be excellent and verifiable, but the expansion path is clear: multi-tenant productionization, warehouse-agnostic adapters behind the semantic layer, and eventually true causal inference â€” difference-in-differences and uplift modeling â€” plus a closed loop that pushes experiment cards to a testing platform, reads back the result, and updates the backlog. I'm honest that the public dataset is observational and three months long, so today Helios *designs and sizes* experiments and runs quasi-experimental readbacks rather than live A/Bs. The architecture is built to absorb live experimentation the moment the data supports it."

### Why Helios exists â€” the core arguments

**Why Helios exists.** Modern e-commerce orgs are drowning in descriptive signal and starving for diagnostic answers. A dashboard can show `session_conversion_rate` fell from 2.1% to 1.7%, but cannot say why, cannot price it, and cannot prescribe a fix. Helios exists to close that insight-to-action gap â€” to make the question "why did the funnel move?" answerable by an artifact that already exists, is grounded in governed SQL, is defended statistically, and has survived an adversarial critique, rather than by a Slack ping that kicks off two days of ad-hoc querying.

**What problem it solves.** It automates root-cause analysis of the conversion funnel and makes every finding actionable. Concretely: detect the anomaly, decompose mix vs rate vs interaction, verify the root cause against governed data, quantify revenue-at-risk in dollars, and prescribe a powered experiment â€” collapsing a one-to-three-day manual process to under five minutes, while structurally preventing the two failure modes that wreck manual RCA: hallucinated metrics and confusing composition change with behavior change.

**Why dashboards are insufficient.** Dashboards report *what*, never *why* â€” no causal-style attribution, no mix/rate split. They are pull, not push: someone must notice and ask. They are blind to interaction confounds across dimensions like `channel_group Ã— device_category`. They carry no dollar quantification, so a percentage-point drop never becomes a budget decision. And they prescribe nothing â€” no experiment, no power analysis, no prioritized backlog. A dashboard is the *input* to diagnosis, not diagnosis.

**Why autonomous diagnosis matters.** A human analyst is a serial, reactive oracle queried only after someone notices a number looks off â€” which can be days later. Autonomy inverts that latency: diagnosis becomes a continuous background process that runs whether or not anyone is watching, the way CI runs on every commit. Proactive beats reactive because the cost of an undiagnosed week compounds, and because a scheduled, governed, repeatable run is something a team can *trust* in a way they can never trust an ad-hoc one-off.

**Why MCP was chosen.** Model Context Protocol lets me split capability across servers and enforce hard boundaries the LLM cannot cross. `semantic-mcp` is the *only* path to SQL and `stats-mcp` the *only* path to math, with per-agent tool allow-lists â€” the Narrator literally cannot call `run_query`. That capability isolation *is* the trust argument made structural: hallucinated columns and hand-computed statistics aren't policed after the fact, they're impossible by construction. MCP turns "please don't write bad SQL" into "you cannot write SQL at all."

**Why an agent architecture was chosen.** Diagnosis is genuinely multi-step and benefits from specialization and adversarial review â€” a single monolithic prompt can't both confidently propose a root cause *and* rigorously refute it. So I use seven agents on a deterministic finite-state machine: the LLM composes tool calls at each node, but the *flow* is code, not model-controlled. That gives me the reasoning flexibility of agents (a best-first hypothesis tree, an adversarial Critic that can downgrade or drop a finding) with the reproducibility and cost control of a fixed pipeline â€” Opus for orchestration and critique where judgment matters, Sonnet for high-volume sub-tasks.

---

## Section 2: Elevator Pitches

Five ready-to-speak scripts. Each tier strictly adds depth on top of the previous one â€” pick by the time you actually have.

### 30 seconds â€” thesis + wedge

"Helios is an autonomous AI Growth Analyst. Instead of another 'ask your data' chatbot, it runs on a schedule and proactively diagnoses *why* an e-commerce funnel moved â€” and it nails the one thing analysts get wrong: it separates a real behavior change from a mere shift in traffic mix. Every finding ships with a significance test, a dollar revenue-at-risk number, and a recommended experiment. On a labeled benchmark it hits 85%+ root-cause accuracy versus 45% for the naive approach."

### 1 minute â€” + the problem + how it works at a glance

"Helios is an autonomous AI Growth Analyst. The problem it solves: when a funnel metric moves, a human analyst spends one to three days finding the root cause â€” and routinely confuses *mix-shift* (traffic composition changed) with *rate-change* (in-segment behavior changed), which is Simpson's paradox and funds the wrong fix.

Helios runs on a schedule and, in under five minutes, does the whole loop: detect the anomaly, decompose the move into mix versus rate versus interaction, diagnose the root-cause segment, price it in dollars of revenue-at-risk, and prescribe a powered experiment â€” then ships an executive Decision Brief before anyone asks. The decomposition is the centerpiece: it tells you whether conversion dropped because behavior got worse or because your traffic mix shifted toward lower-converting segments. On a labeled benchmark, 85%+ root-cause accuracy versus a 45% naive baseline."

### 3 minutes â€” + architecture + the trust pillars

"[Open with the 1-minute pitch, then continue:]

Under the hood it's five MCP servers and seven agents. The five MCP servers are the trust boundary. `warehouse-mcp` is the sole BigQuery client, with a mandatory dry-run byte-budget gate before any query and a reconcile check against canonical totals. `semantic-mcp` is the *only* path to SQL â€” it composes validated queries from a governed registry of 47 metrics and 19 dimensions, so the model references metric *names* like `view_to_cart_rate`, never raw columns. `stats-mcp` is the *only* path to math â€” decomposition, significance, forecasting, cohort, RFM. `experiment-mcp` does power analysis and experiment design. `report-mcp` renders the brief and handles memory.

The seven agents run on a deterministic state machine: an Orchestrator on Opus; a Monitor that detects anomalies; a Decompose agent that runs the mix/rate math; a Diagnose agent on Opus that builds a best-first hypothesis tree and SQL-verifies each branch; a Critic on Opus that adversarially tries to refute every finding; a Prescribe agent that builds experiment cards; and a Narrator that writes the brief. The LLM composes the tool calls â€” it never controls the flow.

That gives me three defensibility pillars. One, **governed-SQL grounding** â€” the semantic layer is the only SQL author, so hallucinated columns are structurally impossible. Two, **deterministic statistics** â€” all math is real code, never generated tokens. Three, **adversarial verification plus offline eval** â€” the Critic refutes findings, and a labeled benchmark grades accuracy in CI. The thesis is that the hard part of an AI analyst isn't generating insights; it's making them correct and *trusted*."

### 5 minutes â€” + the eval, results, and honest tradeoffs

"[Open with the 3-minute pitch, then continue:]

The reason I can claim 85% isn't a vibe â€” it's an offline labeled benchmark. The public GA4 dataset has real anomalies but they're *unlabeled*, so I can't grade against them. Instead I clone a baseline window into an eval dataset and surgically inject anomalies with a known ground truth, then make the pipeline rediscover what I hid. A *rate* perturbation multiplies one segment's conversion rate while holding its volume fixed â€” ground truth is rate-change. A *mix* perturbation multiplies a segment's sessions while holding rates fixed â€” ground truth is mix-shift. Because totals are conserved, the analytic mix/rate/interaction split is exact and becomes the gold target.

The benchmark is 50 scenarios across seven buckets: single- and multi-segment rate changes, single- and multi-segment mix shifts, seasonality decoys the system must *not* flag, no-anomaly controls that test false-positive rate, and data-quality artifacts the Critic must catch as data issues rather than behavior. The contract: top-1 root-cause accuracy â‰¥85% versus â‰¤45% for a naive 'largest absolute segment delta' baseline; decomposition error â‰¤10% MAPE; 0 hallucinated columns; 100% of findings carrying a significance test and a dollar figure; under five minutes per run; and cost under a fixed 5 GiB BigQuery byte budget. CI gates regressions on every PR.

Now the honest tradeoffs, because they read as maturity. The dataset is observational and historical â€” about three months, late 2020 â€” so Helios *designs and sizes* experiments and runs quasi-experimental readbacks like difference-in-differences; it does not run live A/Bs. That short window means true LTV and long-horizon retention are deferred to short-horizon proxies with stated assumptions. `user_id` is almost always null, so identity is cookie-grain and user-level metrics are explicit cookie approximations. And there's a classic GA4 gotcha: event-level `traffic_source` is *first-touch*, not session source â€” I derive session-scoped source/medium and fall back to first-touch only when null. I'd rather state these limits plainly than have an interviewer find them."

### 10 minutes â€” deep dive: one autonomous run end-to-end, decisions, limits, roadmap

"[Open with the 5-minute pitch, then walk one run end-to-end:]

**One run, end to end.** The scheduler fires. The **Monitor** agent pulls a canonical series â€” say `session_conversion_rate` â€” through `semantic-mcp` (which composes the SQL) and `warehouse-mcp` (which dry-runs the cost, executes, and reconciles to within 0.5%), then calls `stats-mcp.detect_anomaly`. It flags that conversion fell week-over-week. The **Decompose** agent then calls `stats-mcp.decompose_change` across canonical dimensions like `channel_group` and `device_category`. Suppose the result is: per-segment rates are essentially flat, but there's a large *mix* effect â€” a surge of low-converting mobile, Paid Social traffic shifted the weights. That's the Simpson's-paradox moment: the naive read was 'checkout is broken'; the decomposition says behavior didn't change at all, the mix did.

The **Diagnose** agent (Opus) takes that and builds a best-first hypothesis tree, verifying each branch with governed SQL â€” confirming the mobile/Paid Social volume spike is real and localizing it. It dollarizes: affected sessions Ã— rate delta Ã— revenue-per-session, attributed by segment, yields revenue-at-risk. Then the **Critic** (Opus) attacks the finding: is this a mix confound? Is the sample big enough for significance? Could it be a known seasonal swing â€” post-holiday in this dataset? Is it a data-quality artifact like a NULL spike or a late shard? It issues a verdict â€” PASS, DOWNGRADE, or DROP. Only survivors proceed. The **Prescribe** agent (Sonnet) sizes the fix â€” here, an acquisition-mix intervention, not a checkout fix â€” via `experiment-mcp.power_analysis` and `runtime_estimate`. Finally the **Narrator** renders the Decision Brief through `report-mcp`, and the diagnosis is saved to memory so the next run can recall it and track whether the recommended action actually worked. Findings hand off between agents as a typed JSON 'Finding' envelope.

**Key design decisions and the alternatives I rejected.** First, a deterministic state machine over a free-roaming agent â€” I traded some autonomy for reproducibility, cost control, and the ability to put a hard Critic gate in the flow. Second, MCP capability isolation over prompt instructions â€” 'you may not call run_query' enforced by tool allow-lists beats 'please write good SQL,' so the Narrator structurally cannot touch the warehouse. Third, an Opus/Sonnet split â€” Opus where judgment matters (orchestrate, diagnose, critique), Sonnet for high-volume sub-tasks, to keep cost sane. Fourth, deterministic stats in real Python over LLM-computed math â€” the decomposition is unit-tested against hand-worked golden values, because a keystone transform that's silently wrong corrupts every downstream number. Fifth, the offline injection benchmark over eyeballing live anomalies â€” it's the only way to get labeled ground truth and a defensible accuracy number.

**Limitations, stated honestly.** Observational, three-month, late-2020 data: experiments are designed and quasi-experimentally read back, not run live; LTV and long retention are proxied; identity is cookie-grain because `user_id` is null; channel attribution navigates the first-touch `traffic_source` gotcha. The decomposition is correlational, not causal â€” which is exactly why the Critic attaches explicit confound caveats rather than overclaiming.

**Roadmap.** Phase 3 is productionization: multi-tenant isolation, near-real-time streaming ingestion, and warehouse-agnostic adapters behind `warehouse-mcp` so the semantic layer stays the only SQL author across Snowflake, Databricks, or DuckDB. Phase 4 is the frontier: replace correlational decomposition with true causal inference â€” difference-in-differences, synthetic control, double-ML â€” where data supports it; join GA4 with CRM and cost data for true CAC and ROAS; and close the loop by pushing experiment cards to a real experimentation platform, reading results back, and auto-updating the backlog. The architecture was built to absorb each of those without touching the trust spine."

---

## Section 3: Product Analytics Interview

This section covers the three workhorse frameworks of product analytics - funnels, retention, and cohorts - at the depth an interviewer expects from someone who has shipped funnel diagnosis to production. Throughout, I tie the concepts to Helios, where funnel logic lives in `fct_funnel` with monotonic `reached_*` flags, and retention/cohort math is computed deterministically by `stats-mcp.cohort_retention` under an explicit short-horizon caveat.

### Funnels

#### Q1. What is the difference between step (step-to-step) conversion and overall conversion, and why does an analyst need both?
**Ideal answer.** Overall conversion is `purchasing_sessions / sessions` - the end-to-end yield of the whole funnel - while step conversion is the rate between two adjacent stages, e.g. `add_to_cart_sessions / view_item_sessions`. Overall conversion tells you *how much* business you're losing; step conversion tells you *where* you're losing it, because the overall rate is the product of all step rates (`overall = step1 x step2 x ... x stepN`). I report both because a flat overall number can hide a single catastrophic step (a checkout that 90%-abandons), and conversely a single bad step rate is meaningless without knowing how many sessions actually reach it. The discipline is: watch overall as the headline KPI, then attribute movement to the specific step whose rate moved most.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios materializes both `session_conversion_rate` (overall) and the four step rates (`view_to_cart_rate`, `cart_to_checkout_rate`, etc.) in the semantic layer, so the Decompose agent can decompose a movement in the headline rate down to the exact step that caused it before any hypothesis is formed.
**Follow-ups.** If overall conversion is flat but two step rates moved in opposite directions, how do you surface that? / Why is multiplying step rates not exactly equal to overall conversion in real data? / How would you weight which step to investigate first?

#### Q2. Explain ordered vs unordered funnels. When is each appropriate?
**Ideal answer.** An ordered funnel requires users to complete steps in a specific sequence and counts a step only if the prior steps happened first (view -> cart -> checkout -> purchase); an unordered funnel counts whether each event occurred at all within the window, regardless of sequence. Ordered funnels model genuine sequential processes like e-commerce checkout where order is causally meaningful, and they're stricter, so they report lower, more honest conversion. Unordered funnels suit feature-adoption questions ("did the user do A, B, and C in any order this week?") where sequence is irrelevant. The trap is using an unordered count for an inherently ordered process: you'll over-count "conversions" because a user who purchased via a deep link without viewing an item still satisfies an unordered "viewed AND purchased" filter. For a purchase funnel I default to ordered.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's macro funnel (`session_start -> view_item -> add_to_cart -> begin_checkout -> add_shipping_info -> add_payment_info -> purchase`) is strictly ordered and enforced by the `reached_*` max-downstream flags, so a session marked `reached_begin_checkout` provably passed through the earlier stages.
**Follow-ups.** How do you handle a user who skips a step (e.g. one-click buy)? / What breaks if you compute an ordered funnel without a per-user timestamp ordering? / When would you deliberately relax ordering?

#### Q3. How do you choose the conversion window for a funnel, and what bias does the choice introduce?
**Ideal answer.** The window is the time allowed for a user to complete downstream steps after entering, and it should match the natural decision latency of the action: minutes-to-hours for an impulse e-commerce purchase, days-to-weeks for a high-consideration B2B signup. Too short a window under-counts conversions that legitimately complete later (truncation bias); too long inflates conversion by crediting unrelated later sessions and makes the funnel slow to react to recent changes. The right approach is to inspect the empirical distribution of time-to-convert and set the window at a high percentile (e.g. p90) of genuine conversions, then hold it fixed so trends are comparable. I also distinguish *session-scoped* funnels (window = one session) from *user-scoped* funnels (window spans sessions), since they answer different questions.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios scopes its funnel to a single session - session key `(user_pseudo_id, ga_session_id)` - which makes the conversion window unambiguous (intra-session) and sidesteps cross-session attribution noise, a deliberate simplification stated as a known limitation rather than hidden.
**Follow-ups.** How would you measure time-to-convert to justify the window? / What changes if you move from session-scoped to 7-day user-scoped? / How does window choice interact with seasonality?

#### Q4. A funnel step's drop-off jumped last week. Walk me through how you attribute the cause.
**Ideal answer.** First I confirm the drop is real and not noise - is the change statistically significant given the sample reaching that step, or is it a small-N artifact? Then I decompose the rate change: did the *segments' behavior* worsen (rate-change), or did the *traffic mix* shift toward inherently lower-converting segments (mix-shift)? These have completely different fixes - rate-change points at a product/UX/pricing defect, mix-shift points at acquisition. I then segment the step by device, channel, geo, and landing page to localize the regression, and cross-check against deploy logs, tracking changes, and known launches before concluding. Finally I quantify the impact in dollars (sessions lost x downstream value) so the finding is prioritized by money, not by percentage-point drama.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** This is Helios's core loop: `stats-mcp.significance_test` gates the anomaly, `stats-mcp.decompose_change` splits the movement into `mix_effect` / `rate_effect` / `interaction`, and the Diagnose agent drills the rate effect first because that's where real behavior change lives.
**Follow-ups.** What's your significance test for a proportion change? / How do you rule out a tracking/data-quality regression? / How do you convert a step drop into revenue-at-risk?

#### Q5. What is mix-shift vs rate-change in a funnel, and why does conflating them cause wrong decisions?
**Ideal answer.** For an aggregate rate `R = sum_i (w_i * r_i)` where `w_i` is a segment's volume share and `r_i` its conversion rate, a change decomposes as `mix = sum(dw_i * r_i(t0))`, `rate = sum(w_i(t0) * dr_i)`, and `interaction = sum(dw_i * dr_i)`. Mix-shift means the aggregate moved purely because traffic composition changed (e.g. a paid-social campaign flooded the top of funnel with low-intent mobile users) even though *every segment's* conversion held steady; rate-change means in-segment behavior genuinely changed. Conflating them is the classic error: you "fix" a checkout you think is broken when actually the checkout is fine and acquisition got cheaper-and-worse. Decomposing first tells you whether to call product or marketing.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** This decomposition identity *is* Helios's technical centerpiece - `stats-mcp.decompose_change` computes it deterministically in Python (never in token-space), which is exactly how Helios dissolves Simpson's paradox before forming any hypothesis.
**Follow-ups.** Give an example where overall conversion rises but every segment's rate falls. / Why compute this in real code rather than letting an LLM estimate it? / How big does interaction have to be before you report it separately?

#### Q6. Why can an aggregate funnel mislead you (Simpson's paradox), and how do you guard against it?
**Ideal answer.** Simpson's paradox is when a trend present in every subgroup reverses or vanishes in the pooled data because the subgroup *weights* changed. In funnels this means overall conversion can rise while mobile, desktop, and tablet conversion each fall - simply because desktop (the higher-converting segment) gained share. The aggregate is a weighted average, and weighted averages move for two reasons: the weights or the rates. The guard is to never trust a pooled rate movement on its own: always decompose into mix vs rate, and always compute rates as `SUM(numerator)/SUM(denominator)` after grouping rather than averaging per-segment ratios, which silently re-weights every segment to equal.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios bakes this in two places: the dbt convention that rates are `SUM(num)/SUM(den)` post-group (never a mean of ratios), and `decompose_change` as a mandatory step so no finding ships on an un-decomposed aggregate.
**Follow-ups.** Why does averaging per-segment ratios re-weight the data? / Which dimension would you decompose by first and why? / How do you present a Simpson reversal to a non-technical exec?

#### Q7. How do you analyze a funnel by segment without drowning in dimension combinations?
**Ideal answer.** I treat segmentation as a guided search, not a full cross-join. Start with the single dimension whose decomposition contributes the largest *rate effect* to the movement, drill into its worst-performing level, then conditionally expand to a second dimension only within that branch - a best-first traversal rather than enumerating device x channel x geo x landing-page exhaustively. I cap segment cardinality and enforce a minimum sample per cell so I'm not chasing 12-session segments with noisy rates. The output is a short, ranked path ("Paid Social -> mobile -> /sale landing page") rather than a 400-row pivot, because a prioritized narrative is what drives action.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The Diagnose agent runs a best-first hypothesis-tree RCA over Helios's conforming dimensions (`device_category`, `channel_group`, `country`, etc.), expanding only the most promising branch and SQL-verifying each node, which keeps it inside the 5 GiB byte budget instead of blowing it on a full cube.
**Follow-ups.** What minimum sample do you require per segment cell? / How do you stop the tree from over-fitting to one noisy segment? / How do you handle high-cardinality dimensions like landing_page?

#### Q8. How would you design and QA a funnel data model so step rates are never greater than 1 and never silently wrong?
**Ideal answer.** The invariant is monotonicity: `sessions >= reached_step1 >= reached_step2 >= ... >= reached_purchase`. I enforce it by defining each step flag as "reached this step *or any later step*" (a max-downstream flag) computed from the maximum stage achieved per session, rather than independent per-event booleans that can produce a later step with no earlier one. Then every step rate is guaranteed `<= 1` by construction. I QA it with tests asserting the monotonic chain and with a reconciliation that the funnel's terminal count matches the orders fact to the cent. These transforms fail *silently* - a subtly wrong sessionization poisons every downstream number - so they get golden-value tests, not just spot checks.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's `int_ga4__funnel_steps` uses `reached_*` monotonic flags (the `did_*` naming is retired precisely because it allowed non-monotonic states), and revenue at the terminal step reconciles against `fct_orders` via the `revenue_reconciles` test - the keystones flagged as silent-failure risks.
**Follow-ups.** Show the SQL shape of a max-downstream flag. / What test catches a sessionization bug before it reaches the funnel? / Why is reconciliation to <=0.5% drift part of the funnel's definition of done?

### Retention

#### Q9. Define classic (N-day), rolling, and bracket (range) retention. When do you use each?
**Ideal answer.** Classic N-day retention measures the share of a cohort active *exactly* on day N after acquisition - precise but harsh and spiky for low-frequency products. Rolling (a.k.a. "N-day or later" / unbounded) retention counts users active on day N *or any day after*, which is smoother and better for products used irregularly. Bracket/range retention buckets activity into windows (day 0, days 1-7, days 8-30) and counts a user retained if active anywhere in the bracket - the practical default for weekly or monthly products because it tolerates natural usage cadence. The choice must match usage frequency: applying classic day-7 retention to a product people use monthly will report near-zero retention that's an artifact of the metric, not the product.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios exposes retention through `stats-mcp.cohort_retention` as a deterministic, parameterized computation, so the retention *definition* (classic vs rolling vs bracket) is an explicit, auditable argument rather than an ad-hoc SQL decision buried in a query.
**Follow-ups.** Which would you pick for a monthly-use product and why? / How does rolling retention relate to a survival/Kaplan-Meier curve? / What's the failure mode of classic retention for low-frequency apps?

#### Q10. What does a retention curve look like, and what do its shape, slope, and floor tell you?
**Ideal answer.** A retention curve plots the fraction of a cohort still active against days/weeks since acquisition. Healthy products show a steep initial drop (the "shedding" of low-intent signups) that then *flattens into a non-zero asymptote* - the "smile" or plateau - which is the retained core that drives long-term value. A curve that decays to zero with no floor means no durable habit formed and the product is leaky no matter how much you spend on acquisition. The two things I read are the *floor* (does it asymptote above zero?) and the *slope after the elbow* (a slowly declining plateau still erodes the base). A rising or "smiling" curve, where later periods recover, signals genuine product-market fit.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios computes the per-period retained fractions via `cohort_retention` so the curve's elbow and floor are quantified outputs, but it explicitly caps interpretation at the ~3-month data window - it will not extrapolate a plateau it cannot observe.
**Follow-ups.** How do you distinguish a true plateau from not-enough-data? / What slope on the plateau still worries you? / How would you compare two cohorts' curves statistically?

#### Q11. N-day vs unbounded retention: what's the trade-off and which is more honest?
**Ideal answer.** N-day (exact-day) retention is unbiased for the precise question "active on day N" but is high-variance and punishes any product not used daily, often understating real engagement. Unbounded ("day N or later") retention is smoother and arguably more honest about whether a user is *still a user*, but it's monotonically non-increasing only in N and can mask declining frequency because a user who logs in once on day 60 counts as retained through day 30. Neither is universally "right" - the honest move is to state the definition explicitly and pick the one whose question matches the decision. For churn/health monitoring I lean unbounded; for measuring a specific day-1 onboarding intervention I use exact N-day so the signal isn't diluted by later activity.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Because retention is a tool argument in `stats-mcp.cohort_retention`, Helios forces the definition to be named and stored with the diagnosis in memory, so a later run reads back retention computed the same way - no silent redefinition between runs.
**Follow-ups.** Why is unbounded retention monotonic in N? / When does unbounded retention hide a real decline? / Which do you put on the exec dashboard?

#### Q12. Why is retention often called the truest signal of product health - truer than acquisition or even revenue?
**Ideal answer.** Acquisition and top-line revenue can be bought - a spend spike inflates both without any improvement in the product - whereas retention measures whether users came *back* unprompted, which is the closest observable proxy for delivered value and the engine of compounding growth. Retention sets the ceiling on LTV (LTV is essentially the integral under the retention curve times per-period value), so a leaky bucket caps every other metric no matter how good the funnel is. It's also lagging and hard to game, which is exactly what makes it trustworthy. The mature framing: funnels tell you if you can *get* users, retention tells you if you *deserve* them.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios prices movements in dollars of revenue-at-risk and prescribes experiments, but it's explicit that on a ~3-month observational window it reports *short-horizon retention proxies with stated assumptions* rather than claiming true LTV - treating retention as the health signal while being honest about how far it can see.
**Follow-ups.** How is LTV related to the area under the retention curve? / Why is retention harder to game than revenue? / What short-horizon proxy would you trust on 3 months of data?

#### Q13. What is survivorship bias in retention analysis and how does it distort conclusions?
**Ideal answer.** Survivorship bias is drawing conclusions from only the users who "survived" to the point of measurement while ignoring those who churned earlier - so the surviving population looks healthier and more engaged than the original cohort ever was. It shows up when you compute "average engagement of active users" over time: the average rises not because anyone improved but because the disengaged dropped out, leaving a denominator of self-selected loyalists. Another form is analyzing only cohorts old enough to have "matured," which excludes recently-acquired (and possibly worse-retaining) users. The fix is to always anchor metrics to the *original cohort denominator at acquisition*, and to be explicit when a metric conditions on still being active.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** `cohort_retention` always divides by the cohort's *acquisition-time* size, not the surviving count, so retained fractions can't drift upward purely from attrition - and the Critic agent flags "insufficient sample" / survivorship-style confounds as a refutation category before a finding ships.
**Follow-ups.** Where does "average sessions per active user" hide survivorship? / How do you correct an engagement metric for it? / How does the Critic catch this class of error?

#### Q14. How would you statistically compare retention between two cohorts (e.g. before vs after a change)?
**Ideal answer.** I compare retention at matched periods-since-acquisition (day-7 vs day-7), not calendar dates, and test the difference in retained proportions with a two-proportion z-test or chi-square, reporting a confidence interval on the gap, not just a point delta. For the whole curve I'd use a log-rank test on the survival curves so I'm comparing shapes rather than one cherry-picked day. Crucially I check that the cohorts are comparable on mix (channel, device, geo) first - a "retention improvement" that's really a mix-shift toward better-retaining channels is the same Simpson trap as in funnels. Because this is observational data, I frame it as a quasi-experimental pre/post or difference-in-differences readback, not a causal A/B claim.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios routes all such tests through `stats-mcp.significance_test` (deterministic, seeded) and is explicit that on its historical dataset it runs quasi-experimental pre/post and diff-in-diff readbacks rather than live A/Bs - sizing experiments it cannot actually run live.
**Follow-ups.** Why match on period-since-acquisition rather than calendar week? / When is a log-rank test better than a per-day z-test? / How does diff-in-differences strengthen a pre/post retention claim?

#### Q15. A retention curve flattened into a plateau after a release. Is that good, and how do you verify it's real?
**Ideal answer.** A plateau above zero is the goal - it signals a retained core - but after a release I'm suspicious of three alternatives before I celebrate: (1) not enough elapsed time, so the "plateau" is just the youngest cohorts not having had time to churn yet; (2) survivorship, where the denominator quietly shifted; and (3) seasonality, where a holiday lull or spike coincided with the release. I verify by comparing same-age cohorts across the release boundary, checking significance on the plateau-period retention, and decomposing whether the improvement is in-segment (real) or a mix-shift toward stickier channels. Only after those refutations survive do I attribute the plateau to the release.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** This maps directly onto the Critic's adversarial refutation categories - seasonality decoy, insufficient sample, mix-confound, data-quality - which must all return PASS before Helios reports the plateau as a genuine win rather than a DOWNGRADE/DROP.
**Follow-ups.** How do you tell a real plateau from cohort immaturity? / What seasonality control would you use on GA4 holiday data? / Why decompose a retention gain by segment?

#### Q16. How do you handle low-frequency products where day-N retention looks terrible by construction?
**Ideal answer.** For a product genuinely used weekly or monthly, daily retention will look near-zero even with perfect engagement, so I switch the *unit of time* to match cadence - weekly or monthly retention buckets (bracket retention) - and define "active" by a meaningful action, not just a session open. I also look at frequency/recency directly (how often does the core return, and how recently) rather than forcing a daily lens. The mistake is keeping the daily metric and concluding the product is failing; the metric is failing the product. Picking the retention granularity is itself an analytical decision that should be justified from the observed usage-interval distribution.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Because retention granularity is a parameter to `stats-mcp.cohort_retention`, Helios can switch between daily/weekly/monthly bucketing without rewriting SQL - and it pairs retention with RFM-style recency/frequency via `stats-mcp.rfm_segment` for low-frequency populations.
**Follow-ups.** How do you pick weekly vs monthly from the data? / What defines "active" for a low-frequency product? / How does RFM complement a retention curve here?

#### Q17. What's the relationship between retention, churn, and engagement, and how do you avoid double-counting them as separate wins?
**Ideal answer.** Retention and churn are complements at a fixed horizon (period churn = 1 - period retention), so reporting both a "retention up" and a "churn down" headline from the same data is double-counting the same movement. Engagement (depth/frequency of use among the active) is a *different* axis: you can have stable retention with rising engagement, or shrinking-but-deepening usage. The clean framing separates the *width* of the base (retention/churn) from the *intensity* of use (engagement), and recognizes that engagement often *predicts* future retention - so I use engagement as a leading indicator and retention as the confirming lagging metric, never as two independent KPIs to celebrate twice.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios keeps these distinct in its semantic layer - `engagement_rate`/`engaged_sessions` measure intensity while `cohort_retention` measures the durable base - so a Decision Brief never books a single underlying movement as two separate findings.
**Follow-ups.** Give a case where retention is flat but engagement rises - what do you do? / Why is engagement a leading indicator of retention? / How would you test whether engagement predicts next-period retention?

### Cohorts

#### Q18. What is a cohort, and what's the difference between an acquisition cohort and a behavioral cohort?
**Ideal answer.** A cohort is a group of users sharing a defining event in a common time window, analyzed together over their lifetime so you compare like-aged users rather than mixing tenures. An *acquisition* cohort is grouped by *when* they arrived (the January-signups, the week-of-launch users) and answers "is each successive vintage of users better or worse?" A *behavioral* cohort is grouped by *what they did* (users who completed onboarding, users who added a payment method) and answers "does doing X change downstream retention/revenue?" Acquisition cohorts isolate temporal/quality trends in the funnel top; behavioral cohorts isolate the value of specific actions and are how you find activation "aha" moments. The two answer different questions and shouldn't be conflated.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's data model carries `is_new_user` and `session_number_bucket` dimensions plus an acquisition-date grain, so `cohort_retention` can be sliced as an acquisition cohort (by arrival week) or a behavioral one (e.g. sessions that `reached_add_to_cart`) without leaving governed SQL.
**Follow-ups.** Which cohort type finds an activation "aha" moment? / How would you define a behavioral cohort in this GA4 funnel? / What confound threatens a behavioral-cohort conclusion?

#### Q19. How do you read a cohort retention triangle/grid, and what patterns matter?
**Ideal answer.** A cohort grid has one row per acquisition cohort and one column per period-since-acquisition, with the retained fraction in each cell - so you read *down a column* to compare the same age across cohorts (is the day-7 number improving for newer vintages?) and *across a row* to trace one cohort's decay curve. Verticals isolate whether product/onboarding changes lifted later cohorts (cohort-over-cohort improvement at fixed age); diagonals reveal calendar-time shocks (a holiday or outage hitting all live cohorts at once). The single most important read is the column trend at a stable horizon, because that's the apples-to-apples signal that the product is getting stickier independent of how much time has elapsed.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's `cohort_retention` output is exactly this period-since-acquisition grid, and the Decompose/Diagnose agents read columns (same-age cohort-over-cohort) so an improvement isn't faked by simply having older, more-decayed cohorts in the mix.
**Follow-ups.** What does a diagonal pattern tell you vs a vertical one? / Why compare at fixed age rather than fixed calendar date? / How do you spot a tracking change in the grid?

#### Q20. What does "reading cohort-over-cohort" mean and what's the most common mistake?
**Ideal answer.** Reading cohort-over-cohort means comparing successive acquisition cohorts at the *same maturity* - January's day-30 retention vs February's day-30 retention - to judge whether the product is improving for each new vintage. The most common mistake is comparing cohorts at the same *calendar date* instead of the same age: a newer cohort has had less time to churn, so it will always look artificially better, an "immaturity" illusion that evaporates as they age. The second mistake is comparing cohorts of wildly different *composition* (a paid-campaign cohort vs an organic cohort) and attributing a retention gap to the product when it's really an acquisition-mix difference - the same mix-vs-rate problem that haunts funnels.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios anchors cohort comparisons to period-since-acquisition (not calendar) and runs `decompose_change` to separate whether a cohort-over-cohort shift is mix (cohorts sourced differently) or rate (genuinely better retention) before crediting a release.
**Follow-ups.** How does cohort immaturity inflate a young cohort? / How do you control for acquisition-mix differences between cohorts? / What's the minimum elapsed time before a cohort comparison is fair?

#### Q21. Why is the ~3-month data window a serious caveat for cohort and retention conclusions, and how do you handle it honestly?
**Ideal answer.** With only ~3 months of data, the earliest cohort has at most ~90 days of observable history and the latest has almost none, so any long-horizon retention or LTV claim is extrapolation beyond what the data can support - the curve's true asymptote and tail are simply unobserved. The honest handling is to (1) report only short-horizon proxies (e.g. day-7, day-30 retention) and label them as such, (2) state the assumptions any projection rests on rather than presenting a smooth LTV number, and (3) restrict cohort-over-cohort comparisons to ages every compared cohort has actually reached. Pretending to a 12-month LTV from 3 months of data is exactly the over-claiming that erodes trust with a senior stakeholder.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** This is one of Helios's explicit maturity points: the dataset is observational and ~3 months (Nov 2020-Jan 2021), so Helios *defers true LTV*, reports short-horizon retention proxies with stated assumptions, and won't extrapolate a tail it can't see.
**Follow-ups.** What horizon would you trust on this dataset and why? / How would you caveat an LTV projection to an exec? / How does limited history interact with cohort immaturity?

#### Q22. How would you build an acquisition-cohort retention analysis on GA4 data, given user_id is mostly null?
**Ideal answer.** I'd grain the cohort on the first-seen date per identity, but I have to be explicit that GA4's `user_id` is almost always null, so identity is cookie-grain (`user_pseudo_id`) - meaning these are *cookie-based* cohorts that over-count users who clear cookies or switch devices and can't stitch cross-device journeys. I assign each `user_pseudo_id` to its acquisition week, then compute retained fractions at each period-since-acquisition, comparing cohorts down the column. I'd state the cookie-grain assumption up front because it caps the precision of any user-level retention claim, and I'd avoid implying these are true deduplicated humans. Naming the limitation is what makes the analysis credible.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios is explicit that `user_id` is almost always null so identity is cookie-grain (`user_pseudo_id`) and user-level metrics are cookie-based approximations - the cohort math in `cohort_retention` inherits and surfaces that caveat rather than hiding it.
**Follow-ups.** How does cookie-grain identity bias retention up or down? / What would change with stitched cross-device identity? / How do you communicate "cookie-based, not user-based" to a PM?

#### Q23. How do you decide cohort granularity (daily vs weekly vs monthly) and cohort size?
**Ideal answer.** Granularity trades resolution against statistical stability: daily cohorts give fine temporal detail but small, noisy cells; monthly cohorts are stable but blur fast-moving changes. I pick the grain from two things - the product's natural usage cadence and the per-cohort sample size needed for the retained-fraction confidence intervals to be tight enough to act on. On a short window with moderate traffic, weekly cohorts are usually the sweet spot: enough users per cohort to be significant, enough cohorts to see a trend. I also align cohort width to the retention bucket width so I'm not comparing daily cohorts on monthly retention or vice versa, which mismatches the two time axes.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios carries both `day` and `week` dimensions, and because cohort grain and retention bucketing are parameters to `cohort_retention`, it sizes cohorts to keep per-cell samples above the threshold the `significance_test` needs - all under the fixed 5 GiB byte budget.
**Follow-ups.** How does cohort size affect the confidence interval on retention? / Why align cohort width with retention-bucket width? / What sample floor do you set per cohort cell?

#### Q24. How would you find an "aha moment" / activation metric using behavioral cohorts, and what's the causal caveat?
**Ideal answer.** The standard play is to build behavioral cohorts on candidate early actions (added an item, completed checkout setup, returned within 24h) and compare downstream retention between users who did vs didn't do each action within their first session/week - the action with the largest, most stable retention lift is the activation candidate. The non-negotiable caveat is that this is *correlational*: motivated users both perform the action and retain, so the action may be a marker of intent rather than a cause. To move toward causation I'd design an experiment that nudges the action for a random subset, or at minimum run a quasi-experimental matched comparison and stress-test for the obvious confounds before recommending the team optimize for it.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's Prescribe agent turns such a candidate into a *powered* experiment card (via `experiment-mcp.power_analysis`/`design_experiment`) precisely because Helios refuses to present a behavioral-cohort correlation as a causal lever without sizing the experiment to confirm it.
**Follow-ups.** Why is a behavioral-cohort retention lift not proof of causation? / How would you design an experiment to confirm the aha moment? / What confounds would the Critic raise here?

#### Q25. Tie it together: how do funnels, retention, and cohorts combine into a single growth diagnosis?
**Ideal answer.** They're three lenses on one system and most powerful when chained. The funnel localizes *where* in the session conversion breaks and prices the leak in dollars; the mix-vs-rate decomposition says whether to fix product or acquisition; cohorts then ask whether the leak is a one-time shock (a diagonal calendar event) or a structural decline in successive vintages (a worsening column); and retention answers whether the users you *do* convert actually stick - because fixing a funnel that feeds a leaky bucket just spends faster. The senior synthesis is a single prioritized narrative: the largest dollar-impact, statistically-significant, mix-vs-rate-disambiguated finding, with the cohort/retention context that says whether it's acute or chronic, plus a prescribed, powered experiment - all bounded by an honest statement of what the 3-month window can and can't support.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** This is precisely Helios's end-to-end pipeline: Monitor -> Decompose (mix/rate) -> Diagnose (funnel/segment RCA) -> Critic (refute) -> Prescribe (powered card) -> Narrator (Decision Brief), with funnel logic in `fct_funnel`/`reached_*`, cohort/retention math in `stats-mcp`, and the whole chain graded against the offline 85%-vs-45% benchmark.
**Follow-ups.** Which lens do you reach for first on a revenue drop and why? / How do you keep three analyses from producing three disjoint findings? / How does the dollar-impact ranking arbitrate between a funnel fix and a retention fix?

---

### Experimentation

#### Q26. How do you turn a vague product hunch into a testable experiment hypothesis?
**Ideal answer.** A good hypothesis is falsifiable and pre-commits to a direction, a mechanism, and a magnitude: "Because [behavioral insight], if we [change], then [primary metric] will move by [at least the MDE], measured over [window], without harming [guardrail]." I force the mechanism clause because it prevents "we changed the button color and conversion went up" stories that don't generalize â€” the mechanism is what you actually learn. I also pre-register the primary metric, the analysis unit, the segment cuts, and the decision rule before I see data, so I can't retrofit a winner. The single most common failure I avoid is the unfalsifiable hypothesis ("this will improve engagement") that no result could ever contradict. Concretely, a Helios-style hypothesis reads: "Because Paid Search add_to_cartâ†’begin_checkout rate dropped 6pp driven by a mobile rate-change (not mix), if we add an express-checkout entry point, begin_checkout rate recovers â‰¥3pp over 14 days, with bounce rate as a guardrail."
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The Prescribe agent emits experiment cards whose hypothesis is *derived* from the decompose_change rate-vs-mix verdict, so the mechanism clause is grounded in a quantified, SQL-verified diagnosis rather than a guess.
**Follow-ups.** How do you decide the experiment's primary metric vs secondary metrics? / What stops a stakeholder from changing the success metric after seeing results? / When is a directional hypothesis ("X goes up") insufficient and you need a magnitude?

#### Q27. Walk me through sizing an A/B test: MDE, power, significance, and sample size.
**Ideal answer.** Sample size is driven by four levers: baseline rate p, the minimum detectable effect (MDE), significance Î± (false-positive tolerance), and power 1âˆ’Î² (false-negative tolerance, usually 80%). For a two-proportion test the per-arm n â‰ˆ (z_{1âˆ’Î±/2}+z_{1âˆ’Î²})Â² Â· [pâ‚€(1âˆ’pâ‚€)+pâ‚(1âˆ’pâ‚)] / (pâ‚âˆ’pâ‚€)Â². The non-negotiable discipline is to set the MDE from the *smallest business-meaningful lift*, not from "what's detectable in two weeks" â€” sizing to a flattering MDE is how teams ship underpowered tests that can't distinguish a real null from noise. I always convert n into a *runtime* using actual daily eligible traffic and then sanity-check against business calendar (holidays, full weekly cycles). If the runtime is implausibly long, that's signal to widen the MDE, pick a more sensitive proxy metric, or not run the test at all.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** experiment-mcp exposes `power_analysis` and `runtime_estimate` exactly so every Prescribe card carries an MDE, required n per arm, and a feasibility-checked runtime in days against the funnel's measured traffic â€” no card ships "go run an A/B test" without the math attached.
**Follow-ups.** How does variance reduction (CUPED) change your sample-size math? / Why is MDE the lever you should set first, not last? / How do you size a test on a continuous metric like revenue-per-session vs a proportion?

#### Q28. What are guardrail metrics and why do they matter more than the primary metric sometimes?
**Ideal answer.** Guardrail metrics are the metrics you're *not* trying to move but refuse to break â€” latency, error rate, refund rate, unsubscribe rate, overall revenue. They exist because almost any local optimization can shift harm elsewhere: a checkout redesign that lifts begin_checkout but tanks payment success is a net loss disguised as a win. I treat guardrails asymmetrically â€” the primary metric needs to clear a positive MDE, but a guardrail only needs to *not regress* beyond a tolerance, so I run a non-inferiority test on it rather than demanding significance. The maturity point is recognizing that a "winning" experiment with a silently degraded guardrail is the most dangerous outcome, because it ships and the damage compounds. I pre-register guardrails alongside the primary so no one quietly drops the inconvenient one after the fact.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Every Prescribe experiment card names an explicit guardrail (e.g., bounce rate or downstream purchase rate), and the Critic agent will DOWNGRADE a prescription whose proposed change plausibly trades a near-term step-rate gain for a downstream-revenue loss.
**Follow-ups.** How do you set the tolerance band on a guardrail? / What's the difference between a guardrail and a secondary metric? / How do you handle a test that wins on primary but trips a guardrail by a hair?

#### Q29. Explain the peeking problem and how sequential testing addresses it.
**Ideal answer.** Peeking is checking a fixed-horizon test repeatedly and stopping the moment p < 0.05 â€” this inflates the false-positive rate far above the nominal Î± because you get many independent chances to cross the threshold (with daily peeks over two weeks, true type-I error can exceed 30%). The naive fix is "don't look," which is operationally unrealistic. The correct fixes are *sequential* methods that are valid under continuous monitoring: group-sequential designs with alpha-spending functions (O'Brien-Fleming, Pocock), or always-valid inference via mixture sequential probability ratio tests / always-valid confidence sequences. These spend your error budget across looks so the *anytime* false-positive rate stays bounded. The tradeoff is a modest sample-size premium versus a fixed-horizon test in exchange for the freedom to stop early and to look whenever you like.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios runs on observational historical data so it doesn't *run* live sequential tests, but experiment-mcp's design templates pre-commit to a fixed horizon and decision rule precisely to model the discipline that prevents peeking â€” and the offline benchmark's significance gating uses pre-registered thresholds rather than stopping when a number looks good.
**Follow-ups.** Why does daily peeking inflate type-I error so much? / O'Brien-Fleming vs always-valid confidence sequences â€” when would you pick each? / How does peeking interact with the multiple-comparisons problem across many metrics?

#### Q30. What are novelty and primacy effects, and how do you keep them from fooling you?
**Ideal answer.** Novelty effect is when users over-engage with anything new simply because it's new, inflating early treatment numbers that decay; primacy (change-aversion) is the mirror image â€” existing users resist a change and depress early numbers that recover as they adapt. Both make the *first few days* unrepresentative of steady state. I defend against them by running tests for at least one to two full behavioral cycles (commonly 2+ weeks), plotting the treatment effect *over time* rather than trusting a single pooled estimate, and segmenting new vs returning users since novelty/primacy mostly bite the returning cohort. If the effect curve hasn't stabilized, the test isn't done regardless of significance. The honest read is that a large day-1 lift that decays toward zero is usually novelty, not a durable win.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** This is the same logic as Helios's Critic seasonality/transient check: a finding driven by a short-lived spike gets challenged the same way a novelty-driven experiment result would, and the eval suite includes seasonality-decoy scenarios that punish mistaking a transient bump for a durable shift.
**Follow-ups.** How long is "long enough" and how do you decide? / How would you formally test whether the treatment effect is stable over time? / Why segment new vs returning users when diagnosing novelty?

#### Q31. When should you NOT run an A/B test?
**Ideal answer.** Don't experiment when you can't get adequate power in a reasonable window (low traffic or a rare event makes the required runtime months long); when the change is a one-way door or ethically/legally mandated (you ship it regardless of result); when network/spillover effects violate the stable-unit assumption (marketplace, social, pricing changes leak across arms); when the cost of the experiment exceeds the value of the information; or when the decision is reversible and cheap enough that just shipping and monitoring is faster learning. In those cases I reach for alternatives: quasi-experiments, interrupted time series, holdout geographies, or bandits for low-stakes optimization. The senior move is treating "should we test at all?" as the first question, not "how do we test?" â€” running an underpowered test you'll over-interpret is worse than running none.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios is built on *observational* historical GA4 data where live A/B testing is impossible, so it designs and *sizes* experiments and then performs quasi-experimental readbacks (pre/post, difference-in-differences) â€” it explicitly does not claim to run live A/Bs, which is itself a "when not to experiment" judgment baked into the architecture.
**Follow-ups.** How do network effects break the A/B assumption, and what do you do instead? / How do you value the information from an experiment to decide if it's worth running? / What's your fallback when traffic is too low to power any test?

#### Q32. How do you measure impact when you can't randomize â€” pre/post and difference-in-differences?
**Ideal answer.** With observational data I lean on quasi-experimental designs that approximate a counterfactual. Naive pre/post is the weakest because it confounds the intervention with everything else that changed over time (seasonality, macro trends), so I only trust it with a stable baseline and explicit seasonality controls. Difference-in-differences is stronger: I compare the treated unit's beforeâ†’after change against an *untreated control* unit's same-period change, which differences out any shock common to both â€” its load-bearing assumption is *parallel trends* (the two would have moved together absent treatment), which I validate on pre-period data. For a single treated series I'll use interrupted time series or a synthetic control (a weighted blend of donor units that reconstructs the pre-period). The honest caveat: these identify effects only under assumptions randomization would have guaranteed for free, so I state those assumptions out loud.
```sql
-- Difference-in-differences on funnel conversion
SELECT
  AVG(IF(treated AND post, rate, NULL)) - AVG(IF(treated AND NOT post, rate, NULL))
    - (AVG(IF(NOT treated AND post, rate, NULL)) - AVG(IF(NOT treated AND NOT post, rate, NULL))) AS did_effect
FROM daily_segment_rates;
```
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Because the GA4 dataset is observational (Nov 2020â€“Jan 2021), Helios's experiment-mcp prescribes the *design* and then reads back impact via pre/post and diff-in-diff rather than asserting causal A/B results â€” and the Critic enforces the parallel-trends / seasonality caveats that keep a pre/post number from being oversold as causal.
**Follow-ups.** How do you validate the parallel-trends assumption before trusting DiD? / When would you choose synthetic control over DiD? / How do you communicate causal uncertainty from a quasi-experiment to an executive?

### North Star Metrics

#### Q33. What makes a good North Star Metric, and how would you choose one for an e-commerce store?
**Ideal answer.** A North Star Metric (NSM) should sit at the intersection of customer value and durable business value, be a leading-ish indicator of revenue (not revenue itself), be measurable today, and be something teams can actually influence. For an e-commerce store I'd resist "revenue" as the NSM because it's a lagging outcome that every team can claim and none can directly move; I'd prefer something like *weekly purchasing customers* or *number of completed purchases per active user* â€” value is realized at purchase, and it captures both acquisition and retention. The test I apply: if this number goes up sustainably for a year, is the business healthier? If yes, and teams can map their work to it, it's a candidate. I pair it with 2â€“4 input metrics so a single number never becomes the only thing the org looks at.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios treats *purchase* as the terminal funnel event and quantifies revenue-at-risk in dollars, so its diagnoses are always anchored to the value-realizing step â€” the NSM-style outcome â€” while decomposing the funnel steps that *lead* to it.
**Follow-ups.** Why not just use revenue as the North Star? / How many input metrics should sit under a North Star? / How would you NSM a pre-monetization product where purchases don't exist yet?

#### Q34. Distinguish leading from lagging indicators with concrete examples.
**Ideal answer.** Lagging indicators measure outcomes after the fact (revenue, churn, LTV, retention) â€” accurate but you can't steer by them in time. Leading indicators move *first* and predict the lagging outcome (add_to_cart rate, week-1 activation, checkout-start rate, repeat-visit frequency), so they're what you actually manage day to day. The art is establishing that the "leading" metric is genuinely predictive of the lagging one rather than just correlated noise â€” I'd validate the lead-lag relationship statistically (e.g., does this week's activation predict next month's retention?) before trusting it. A practical rule: dashboards full of lagging metrics tell you the patient's temperature is high but not why; leading metrics tell you which intervention to make now. I report both, but I *act* on leading and *judge* on lagging.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's funnel is an explicit leadingâ†’lagging chain (session_start â†’ view_item â†’ add_to_cart â†’ â€¦ â†’ purchase); when it diagnoses a purchase shortfall it walks *upstream* to the leading step rate that moved, which is precisely "act on the leading indicator that predicts the lagging outcome."
**Follow-ups.** How do you statistically confirm a metric is leading, not coincident? / Give an example where a leading indicator decoupled from its lagging outcome. / How does the lead time of a leading metric affect how you use it?

#### Q35. What is an input metrics tree and how does it connect daily work to the North Star?
**Ideal answer.** An input metrics tree (or metric tree) decomposes the North Star into the multiplicative or additive drivers a team can directly move, level by level, until you reach metrics that map to specific initiatives. For purchases you might decompose: Purchases = Sessions Ã— (Purchase rate), and Purchase rate = product of step conversion rates across the funnel; each step then fans out into the levers behind it (traffic mix, page performance, friction). The discipline is that the tree must *reconcile* â€” children should roll up to the parent arithmetically â€” so you can attribute a North Star movement to a specific branch. This is what lets a PM owning "checkout friction" see exactly how their step rate flows up to the company NSM. Without the tree, every team optimizes locally and no one can explain why the top number moved.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's semantic layer plus `decompose_change` *is* an operational metric tree: an aggregate rate R = Î£ wáµ¢ráµ¢ is decomposed into mix, rate, and interaction contributions per segment, so a North-Star-level movement is mechanically attributed down the tree to the exact segment and step that caused it.
**Follow-ups.** How do you keep a metric tree reconciling when metrics are ratios? / What happens when two branches of the tree move in opposite directions? / How granular should the leaves of the tree be?

#### Q36. Explain Goodhart's Law and metric gaming â€” how do you design metrics that resist it?
**Ideal answer.** Goodhart's Law: "when a measure becomes a target, it ceases to be a good measure" â€” optimizing a proxy hard enough decouples it from the underlying goal it was standing in for. Classic gaming: a "sessions" target met by splitting one visit into many, or an "add_to_cart" target hit while purchases fall. I design against it with three tactics: pair every target metric with a guardrail or counter-metric that catches the cheap exploit (engagement paired with retention; clicks paired with downstream conversion); prefer metrics closer to true value (purchases over add-to-carts) so the proxy gap is small; and define metrics governed and centrally so a team can't quietly re-spec the numerator to flatter itself. The senior framing is that the more pressure a metric carries, the closer it must sit to real value and the more its definition must be locked down.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's governed semantic layer (semantic-mcp as the *only* SQL path) means no agent or analyst can redefine "purchase rate" to game a result, and the Critic's adversarial refutation explicitly hunts for the "looks good but is an artifact" failure mode that Goodhart-style gaming produces.
**Follow-ups.** How do you choose the counter-metric that catches gaming of a target? / Why does governing a metric's definition reduce gaming? / Give an example where moving a metric closer to true value introduced a new gaming surface.

#### Q37. How do you avoid a single-metric tunnel vision while still having one North Star?
