**Ideal answer.** The North Star is a *focusing* device, not the only number â€” the failure mode is treating it as the sole KPI and optimizing it to the detriment of everything around it. I surround it with a small balanced set: the NSM, its input tree, and a ring of guardrails covering the dimensions the NSM ignores (quality, cost, fairness across segments, long-term retention). I also re-examine the NSM periodically, because the right North Star at one growth stage (activation early, retention/monetization later) is wrong at another. The judgment is holding the tension: enough focus that the org rows together, enough breadth that you notice when "winning" on the NSM is quietly losing the business. A North Star with no guardrails is just a Goodhart trap waiting to spring.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios never reports a single number in isolation â€” its Decision Brief pairs the headline movement with the mix/rate decomposition, revenue-at-risk, *and* the Critic's caveats, so the executive sees the focal metric inside its guardrail context, not alone.
**Follow-ups.** When should an NSM change as the company matures? / How big should the guardrail ring be before it's just a dashboard again? / How do you detect that you're optimizing the NSM at the expense of the business?

#### Q38. How would you validate that your North Star actually predicts long-term business value?
**Ideal answer.** I'd test the predictive link rather than assume it: build a cohort dataset, measure each cohort's early NSM trajectory, and check whether it explains later lagging outcomes (revenue, retention, LTV) â€” correlation over time, lead-lag regression, or out-of-sample prediction. If the NSM doesn't predict the outcome it's supposed to lead, it's the wrong star. I'd also stress it across segments to make sure the relationship isn't a Simpson's-paradox artifact of mix. The honest constraint: predicting *long-term* value needs a long observation window, and with a short dataset I can only validate short-horizon proxies and must state the assumption that the relationship persists. That caveat is part of the answer, not a weakness in it.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios is candid that its ~3-month GA4 window defers true LTV / long-horizon retention, so it reports short-horizon proxies *with stated assumptions* â€” exactly the disciplined honesty required when you can't yet prove the long-term predictive link of a North Star.
**Follow-ups.** What statistical test confirms the NSM predicts the lagging outcome? / How does a short data window limit which North Star you can validate? / How would Simpson's paradox fool you into trusting a bad North Star?

### Segmentation

#### Q39. Explain RFM segmentation and when you'd reach for it.
**Ideal answer.** RFM scores customers on three behavioral axes: Recency (how recently they purchased), Frequency (how often), and Monetary (how much) â€” typically by quintile-ranking each axis and combining the scores into segments like "champions," "at-risk," and "hibernating." It's powerful because it's purely behavioral, computable from transaction logs alone, and maps directly to action (champions get loyalty perks, at-risk get win-back campaigns). I reach for it for retention and CRM/lifecycle targeting where the question is "who do I treat differently and how?" rather than prediction. Its limits: it's descriptive not predictive, it ignores *why* behavior changed, and the quintile cutoffs need to be revisited as the base shifts. I'd use it as a fast, actionable first pass before reaching for a model.
```sql
SELECT user_pseudo_id,
  NTILE(5) OVER (ORDER BY MAX(event_date)) AS r,
  NTILE(5) OVER (ORDER BY COUNT(*))         AS f,
  NTILE(5) OVER (ORDER BY SUM(revenue))     AS m
FROM purchases GROUP BY user_pseudo_id;
```
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** stats-mcp exposes `rfm_segment` as a deterministic, governed primitive, so RFM scoring runs through the same audited stats path as decomposition and significance testing rather than being hand-rolled SQL per analysis.
**Follow-ups.** Why quintiles and not k-means clustering for RFM? / How do you keep RFM cutoffs stable as the customer base grows? / How would you make RFM predictive rather than just descriptive?

#### Q40. Behavioral vs demographic segmentation â€” which do you trust and why?
**Ideal answer.** Demographic segmentation (age, geo, device) is easy to get and easy to explain but often weakly predictive of behavior â€” two 25-year-olds in the same city can have opposite purchase intent. Behavioral segmentation (what users actually *did*: pages viewed, cart actions, purchase cadence, channel) is usually far more predictive and more actionable because it's closer to the moment of value. I default to behavioral for product and lifecycle decisions, and use demographic mainly for acquisition targeting where behavior isn't observable yet. The nuance: demographics are a useful *prior* before behavior accumulates (cold start), and the strongest segmentations combine both. The trap to avoid is treating an easily-measured demographic cut as causal when it's just a proxy for an underlying behavior.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's 16 governed dimensions blend behavioral cuts (funnel step reached, channel group, session source/medium) with contextual ones (device, geo), and its diagnoses prioritize *behavioral* rate-change segments because that's where real, actionable signal lives â€” composition/demographic shifts are flagged as mix effects, not behavior.
**Follow-ups.** When is a demographic segment actually a proxy for a behavior? / How do you segment a brand-new user before any behavior exists? / How would you test which segmentation scheme is more predictive?

#### Q41. What makes a segment actionable, and how do you avoid useless segmentation?
**Ideal answer.** A segment is actionable only if it's (1) measurable from data you have, (2) substantial enough to matter, (3) differentiable â€” members respond differently to a treatment than non-members, and (4) reachable â€” you can actually do something different for them. The classic waste is a statistically tidy segment you can neither target nor treat differently (interesting cluster, no lever), or a segment so small the effort exceeds the payoff. My test before shipping a segmentation: name the *specific different action* each segment gets. If "at-risk users" and "champions" would receive the same treatment, the split earned nothing. I'd rather have three crude, actionable segments than twelve elegant, inert ones.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios always ties a segment-level finding to a *prescribed action* â€” when decompose_change attributes a drop to, say, mobile Paid Search, the Prescribe agent emits an experiment card targeting that exact reachable segment, so segmentation never terminates in an un-actionable cluster.
**Follow-ups.** How small is too small for a segment to be worth treating? / How do you test that a segment is differentiable (responds differently)? / What do you do with an interesting-but-unreachable segment?

#### Q42. What is the "segment of one" / personalization, and what are its tradeoffs?
**Ideal answer.** "Segment of one" is the limit of segmentation â€” personalizing to the individual via models that predict each user's next-best-action, propensity, or recommendation, rather than bucketing into coarse groups. The upside is relevance and lift; the downsides are real: sparse per-user data (most users have thin histories), the cold-start problem, infrastructure and latency cost, harder explainability and governance, and filter-bubble/fairness risks. I treat it as a spectrum, not a binary â€” most value is captured by good behavioral *cohorts*, and true 1:1 is worth it only where data density and stakes justify the complexity. The mature stance is starting with actionable segments and graduating to personalization only where it measurably beats the cohort approach.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios deliberately operates at the cookie-grain segment level (user_pseudo_id, since user_id is almost always null), which is honest about *why* fine-grained per-person personalization isn't credible on this dataset â€” identity is a cookie-based approximation, so segment-level diagnosis is the defensible altitude.
**Follow-ups.** When does a segment-of-one beat well-chosen cohorts enough to justify the cost? / How does sparse per-user data limit personalization? / How would cookie-grain identity (no stable user_id) constrain a personalization system?

#### Q43. How do you decide segmentation granularity without slicing into noise?
**Ideal answer.** Granularity is a bias-variance tradeoff: coarse segments hide real heterogeneity (Simpson's paradox lurks), fine segments fragment into samples too small for stable estimates. I let *sample size and effect stability* set the floor â€” a segment needs enough volume that its rate has a tight enough confidence interval to act on â€” and I let *actionability* set the useful ceiling (don't split finer than you can treat). Practically I'll start coarse, decompose, and only drill into a sub-segment when its contribution is both large and statistically distinguishable from noise. The discipline that prevents over-slicing is requiring significance before trusting a small-segment delta; otherwise you're chasing sampling variance and calling it insight.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's Diagnose agent does best-first hypothesis-tree RCA â€” it drills *rate effects before mix effects* and only deeper into segments that materially move â€” while the Critic explicitly refutes findings built on *insufficient sample*, which is the over-granular-segmentation failure mode.
**Follow-ups.** What's your rule for the minimum sample a segment needs before you trust its rate? / How does Simpson's paradox push you toward finer segmentation? / How do you stop a drill-down from becoming a multiple-comparisons fishing trip?

#### Q44. A segment looks like it's driving a metric change â€” how do you confirm it's real and not a mix artifact?
**Ideal answer.** First I separate two questions: did this segment's *behavior* change (rate), or did its *share of traffic* change (mix)? A naive "largest absolute segment delta" reading conflates them and routinely fingers the wrong cause. I run the mix/rate/interaction decomposition: deltaR = Î£(dwáµ¢Â·ráµ¢) [mix] + Î£(wáµ¢Â·dráµ¢) [rate] + Î£(dwáµ¢Â·dráµ¢) [interaction]; if the segment's contribution is mostly mix, the "driver" is just composition shifting, not behavior. Then I test the rate change for significance so I'm not chasing a small-sample wobble, and I check for confounds (seasonality, a launch, a tracking change). Only a contribution that is rate-driven, significant, and confound-free earns a causal-sounding story. This is exactly how you dissolve Simpson's paradox in attribution.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** This is Helios's technical centerpiece: `decompose_change` deterministically splits a movement into mix/rate/interaction, the Diagnose agent prioritizes the rate-driven (real-behavior) segments, and the Critic adversarially challenges any finding as a possible mix-confound â€” beating a naive baseline 85% vs ~45% on the labeled benchmark.
**Follow-ups.** Walk me through the mix vs rate decomposition arithmetic. / How does a pure mix shift create a Simpson's-paradox reversal? / Why is "largest absolute segment delta" such a dangerous default heuristic?

### Metric Design

#### Q45. Walk me through numerator/denominator discipline in defining a rate metric.
**Ideal answer.** Every rate is a numerator over a denominator, and most metric bugs are denominator bugs â€” the numerator is usually obvious, the denominator is where definitions silently diverge. For "checkout conversion rate," is the denominator sessions that reached checkout, all sessions, or users? Each gives a different number and answers a different question. My discipline: state the unit of analysis explicitly (session vs user vs event), ensure the numerator is a strict subset of the denominator population (you can't have purchases from sessions that aren't in the denominator), align the time windows of both, and define inclusion/exclusion rules (bots, internal traffic, refunds) once. A rate where numerator and denominator are computed over mismatched populations or windows is the single most common way two teams "measure the same metric" and get different answers.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's `reached_*` monotonic max-downstream flags in int_ga4__funnel_steps guarantee numerator/denominator coherence â€” a session that reached purchase is provably counted in every upstream step's denominator â€” so step rates are arithmetically consistent and reconcile to â‰¤0.5%.
**Follow-ups.** Give a case where changing only the denominator flips the conclusion. / How do you align numerator and denominator time windows for a lagging conversion? / Why is the unit of analysis (session vs user) a definitional choice, not a detail?

#### Q46. Why are ratio metrics dangerous, and how does Simpson's paradox show up in them?
**Ideal answer.** Ratio metrics (rates, averages-of-ratios, per-user values) are dangerous because they don't aggregate the way people assume â€” you cannot average two groups' rates to get the pooled rate unless you weight by denominator, and a weighted aggregate can move opposite to every one of its parts. That's Simpson's paradox: each segment's conversion rate can *rise* while the overall rate *falls*, purely because traffic mix shifted toward a lower-converting segment. The defense is to always decompose an aggregate-rate change into mix vs rate effects before interpreting it, and to be explicit about the weighting. Operationally I also avoid "average of per-user ratios" when I mean "ratio of totals" â€” they're different metrics and the per-user-ratio version is heavily distorted by low-denominator users.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Simpson's paradox is the exact pathology Helios was built to dissolve: `decompose_change` mechanically separates the mix component (Î£ dwáµ¢Â·ráµ¢) from the rate component (Î£ wáµ¢Â·dráµ¢), so an aggregate that fell while segments rose is correctly attributed to composition rather than mistaken for behavioral decline.
**Follow-ups.** Show me a numeric example where pooled rate moves opposite to every segment. / When is "ratio of sums" right and "average of ratios" wrong? / How does denominator weighting change which aggregate you should report?

#### Q47. What does it mean for a metric to be "governed," and why does it matter?
**Ideal answer.** A governed metric has a single, version-controlled, centrally-owned definition â€” its SQL logic, unit, filters, and dimensions live in one registry, and every consumer (dashboard, model, agent, report) derives from that definition rather than re-implementing it. It matters because the alternative is *metric drift*: ten teams write ten slightly different "active user" queries, three numbers reach the exec, and the meeting becomes a debate about whose SQL is right instead of what to do. Governance buys consistency, auditability (you can trace any number to its definition), and trust. The tradeoff is upfront cost and some rigidity â€” you can't redefine a metric on a whim â€” but for any metric that drives decisions, that rigidity is the point. The hard part of analytics isn't computing a number; it's everyone computing the *same* number.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's semantic layer (models/semantic/semantic_layer.yaml: 47 metrics + 19 dimensions, referential-integrity compiled, mapping 1:1 to dbt MetricFlow) is the single source of truth, and semantic-mcp's `build_query` is the *only* path to SQL â€” so there are zero hallucinated columns and no agent can author an off-definition metric.
**Follow-ups.** How do you stop teams from re-implementing a metric outside the registry? / What's the cost of governance and when is it not worth it? / How do you migrate a legacy ad-hoc metric into a governed definition?

#### Q48. How do you version and evolve a metric definition without breaking trust in historical numbers?
**Ideal answer.** Metric definitions *must* change (new business logic, fixed bugs, new exclusions), and the danger is silently changing a definition so that a chart's history shifts underneath everyone â€” last quarter's number no longer matches last quarter's deck. I handle it like code: definitions are version-controlled with semantic versioning, changes go through review with a documented rationale, and a breaking redefinition gets a *new* metric version (purchase_rate_v2) rather than mutating v1 in place. I keep a changelog mapping when each version was active, and where possible I backfill both versions during a transition so dashboards can show the discontinuity explicitly rather than hide it. The principle: a metric's history should be reproducible, and any redefinition should be announced and auditable, never silent.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Because Helios's metrics live in a git-versioned semantic_layer.yaml under CI, any definition change is a reviewable diff gated by the offline benchmark â€” the same PR that changes a metric must keep decomposition MAPE â‰¤10% and 0 hallucinated columns, so a redefinition can't silently regress historical correctness.
**Follow-ups.** When do you version-bump a metric vs edit it in place? / How do you communicate a metric redefinition to dashboard consumers? / How would you reproduce a number computed under an old metric version?

#### Q49. Design a metric for "checkout health" from scratch â€” talk me through your reasoning.
**Ideal answer.** I'd start from the decision the metric serves: is checkout *working*, and where does it leak? A single headline rate (begin_checkout â†’ purchase) is the lagging summary, but health needs the step-level breakdown â€” add_shipping_info, add_payment_info, purchase â€” because "checkout is broken" is meaningless without the leaking step. So I'd define checkout health as the conversion rate of each downstream step with the *same* denominator discipline (sessions that reached the prior step), surfaced alongside a guardrail (payment-error rate) and segmented by device and channel since checkout friction is wildly device-dependent. I'd explicitly choose session-grain over user-grain given the data, state the time window, and exclude refunds from revenue. The output isn't one number â€” it's a small governed metric set that localizes the leak and reconciles to the funnel.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** This mirrors Helios's funnel marts exactly: begin_checkout â†’ add_shipping_info â†’ add_payment_info â†’ purchase as monotonic step flags in int_ga4__funnel_steps, each step a governed metric in the semantic layer with consistent denominators, so "checkout health" is decomposable to the exact leaking step and segment.
**Follow-ups.** Why a step-level set rather than one composite checkout score? / How do you pick the denominator for each checkout step? / Which guardrail would you pair with checkout conversion and why?

#### Q50. How do you handle metric definitions that must reconcile across many consumers and stay correct over time?
**Ideal answer.** Reconciliation is an engineering problem, not a willpower problem: I make one definition the source of truth, generate all downstream SQL from it, and add automated checks that the same metric computed two ways agrees within a tolerance. Concretely that means a semantic registry, dry-run cost gates so a query can't silently scan the wrong (more expensive, differently-scoped) tables, and reconciliation tests in CI that fail the build if a metric drifts beyond, say, 0.5%. I also pin the *byte/scope* of each query so "the same metric" can't accidentally read a different partition. The mature recognition is that correctness isn't a one-time verification â€” it's a continuously-enforced invariant, because data, definitions, and consumers all change, and the only durable defense is automation that catches drift on every change.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios enforces exactly this: warehouse-mcp's mandatory dry_run â†’ run_query byte-budget gate with reconcile-to-â‰¤0.5%, the governed semantic layer as the single SQL source, and CI gates that block any PR regressing the offline benchmark â€” so metric correctness is a continuously-enforced invariant, not a hope.
**Follow-ups.** What tolerance do you set for reconciliation and why? / How does a cost/byte gate also protect metric correctness? / What CI checks would you add to catch metric drift before it ships?

---

## Section 4: Growth Analytics Interview

These answers are deliberately framework-based: every response leans on the canonical growth toolkit - AARRR (Acquisition, Activation, Retention, Referral, Revenue), growth loops, the North Star metric, and prioritization scoring (ICE/RICE) - rather than ad-hoc opinion. The thread tying them together is that growth analytics is about *finding the lever that moves a system*, and Helios is the concrete proof: it diagnoses *why* an e-commerce funnel moved by decomposing the AARRR stages it can observe (acquisition mix and activation funnel) into mix-shift vs rate-change.

### Acquisition

#### Q1. Walk me through how you'd evaluate the channel mix of an e-commerce business. What does a healthy mix look like?
**Ideal answer.** Channel mix is the share of sessions (and, more importantly, revenue) coming from each acquisition channel, and I evaluate it on three axes at once: volume share, conversion quality, and cost. A healthy mix is *diversified and not fragile* - no single paid channel should carry the majority of revenue, because that creates a single point of failure when CPMs spike or an algorithm changes - and it skews toward channels with a self-reinforcing growth loop (organic search, direct, referral) rather than purely paid pumps. Concretely I'd group every session into the 10 GA4 default channel groups (Direct, Organic Search, Paid Search, Display, Paid Social, Organic Social, Email, Affiliates, Referral, Other) and compute `sessions`, `session_conversion_rate`, `revenue`, and `revenue_per_session` per group, then look at the *gap* between volume share and revenue share - a channel that is 30% of sessions but 10% of revenue is a quality problem, not a volume win. The senior move is to never average per-channel conversion rates; you compute mix-level rates as `SUM(purchasing_sessions) / SUM(sessions)` after grouping, otherwise Simpson's paradox makes a healthy-looking blended number hide a deteriorating channel.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios pivots every funnel metric across `channel_group` (one of its 16 governed dimensions) sourced from the single `channel_group_case()` macro, so channel-mix analysis is a first-class query, not a one-off; the Decompose agent then splits any channel-level movement into mix vs rate effects.
**Follow-ups.** How would you weight volume vs quality when one channel is high-volume/low-quality? / What blended-mix metric would you put on an executive dashboard? / How does channel mix change the interpretation of a flat overall conversion rate?

#### Q2. Define CAC. What's the difference between blended CAC and paid CAC, and when does each mislead?
**Ideal answer.** CAC (customer acquisition cost) is total acquisition spend over a period divided by the number of *new* customers acquired in that period. Blended CAC divides total spend by *all* new customers including the organically acquired ones, while paid CAC divides paid spend only by customers attributable to paid channels - blended always looks better because organic "free" customers dilute the denominator. Blended CAC misleads when you're trying to decide whether to *scale a paid channel*, because the marginal customer from scaling spend costs the paid CAC (often the marginal CAC, which is higher still), not the flattering blend. Paid CAC misleads in the opposite direction - it ignores that paid spend often *seeds* organic and referral loops (brand search, word-of-mouth), so judging a channel purely on its isolated paid CAC undercounts its halo. The mature framing is to report both, watch the CAC:LTV ratio, and treat blended CAC as the board-level efficiency number and channel-level (marginal) CAC as the budget-allocation number.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios runs on an observational GA4 dataset with no spend data, so it is honest that it cannot compute true CAC; instead it quantifies *revenue-at-risk in dollars* per channel and sizes experiments, which is the half of the unit-economics equation the data actually supports - exactly the kind of scope honesty that reads as maturity.
**Follow-ups.** Why is marginal CAC usually higher than average CAC? / How would payback period change your read on a high-CAC channel? / How do you attribute organic customers that paid spend actually seeded?

#### Q3. A channel doubled its session volume but overall revenue barely moved. How do you diagnose this?
**Ideal answer.** This is the canonical channel-quality-vs-volume problem, and the first thing I'd check is whether the new volume converts at all - I'd decompose the change in `revenue` (or `revenue_per_session`) into a mix-shift component and a rate-change component. If a low-converting channel (say Display or a broad Paid Social campaign) doubled, the *mix* effect alone can move blended `session_conversion_rate` down even though every individual channel's conversion rate is unchanged - that's pure composition. The diagnostic SQL is to pull volume share and per-channel conversion for both periods:
```sql
SELECT channel_group,
       SUM(sessions)                                   AS sessions,
       SUM(purchasing_sessions) / SUM(sessions)        AS conv_rate,
       SUM(revenue) / SUM(sessions)                    AS rev_per_session
FROM helios.marts.fct_daily_funnel
WHERE day BETWEEN @t0_start AND @t1_end
GROUP BY channel_group, period
```
If the doubled channel's `rev_per_session` is far below the others, the volume is real but low-quality and you've simply bought traffic that doesn't buy. The trap to avoid is concluding "conversion is broken" (a rate story) when it's actually a mix story - those have opposite fixes.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** This is the exact scenario Helios was built to disambiguate: `stats-mcp.decompose_change` computes `mix = Î£ Î”w_iÂ·r_i(t0)`, `rate = Î£ w_i(t0)Â·Î”r_i`, and `interaction`, so a "volume doubled, revenue flat" case is resolved deterministically into "mix effect dominates, rates held" rather than a hand-waved guess.
**Follow-ups.** What would the interaction term tell you here? / How would you size the revenue you'd recover by reallocating spend away from the low-quality channel? / When is buying low-converting volume actually rational (e.g., upper-funnel)?

#### Q4. Explain first-touch, last-touch, and last-non-direct attribution. Which would you default to and why?
**Ideal answer.** Attribution assigns credit for a conversion to the touchpoints in the journey: first-touch gives 100% credit to the channel that first acquired the user, last-touch to the final channel before conversion, and last-non-direct (GA4's default) is last-touch but skips direct visits, reassigning credit to the last *campaignable* channel on the theory that "direct" is usually a return visit, not a discovery. I'd default to last-non-direct for day-to-day reporting because it's GA4's native model, it's reproducible, and it avoids over-crediting Direct (which is a junk-drawer bucket - bookmarks, dark social, untagged links). But I'd be explicit that no single-touch model is "correct"; first-touch over-credits discovery channels and starves closers, last-touch does the reverse, and both are wrong for multi-session journeys. For budget decisions I'd want data-driven or position-based multi-touch attribution, and ideally incrementality testing (geo holdouts), because attribution answers "who to credit" while only experiments answer "what was caused."
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios reports against GA4's channel-group conventions and is explicit about Direct being a residual bucket; because the dataset is observational, Helios *designs and sizes* quasi-experimental readbacks (pre/post, difference-in-differences) rather than claiming a single attribution model is causal truth.
**Follow-ups.** Why does Direct inflate under last-touch, and what does that hide? / When does first-touch actually beat last-non-direct? / How would you validate an attribution model against an incrementality test?

#### Q5. There's a well-known GA4 traffic_source gotcha that trips up acquisition analysis. What is it and how do you handle it?
**Ideal answer.** The gotcha is that the event-level `traffic_source` record in the GA4 BigQuery export is the user's *first-touch* source/medium/campaign - it's stamped once at user acquisition and repeats on every event for that user forever - so if you naively `GROUP BY traffic_source.medium`, you are not analyzing *session* acquisition, you're analyzing *user* acquisition, and a user first acquired via Paid Search will have every later organic or direct session mislabeled as Paid Search. This systematically over-credits whatever channel first acquired your users and makes returning-visit channels (Direct, Email) look smaller than they are. The fix is to derive session-scoped source/medium from the `event_params` (the `source`, `medium`, `campaign`, and `gclid` params attached to the session's `session_start`/`page_view` events) and only fall back to the event-level `traffic_source` when the session-scoped params are null. That's the difference between "where did this user originally come from" and "what brought them *this* time," and channel-mix and conversion-by-channel analysis need the latter.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** This is codified as a hard rule in Helios: it derives session-scoped `source`/`medium` from `event_params` and falls back to first-touch `traffic_source` only when null, so its `channel_group` dimension reflects session acquisition, not lifetime first-touch - removing a silent, systematic bias from every acquisition diagnosis.
**Follow-ups.** How would first-touch labeling bias a returning-user cohort analysis? / How do you sessionize source when params are missing mid-session? / What does this gotcha do to a last-non-direct attribution built on top of it?

#### Q6. How do you compare paid vs organic acquisition beyond just cost?
**Ideal answer.** Cost is only one axis; I compare paid vs organic on quality, durability, and scalability. Quality means conversion rate, AOV, and downstream activation/retention of the cohort each channel brings - paid traffic from a broad prospecting campaign often converts and retains worse than organic-search intent traffic, so a "cheaper" paid customer can be more expensive on an LTV basis. Durability is the growth-loop question: organic search, direct, and referral are *compounding* (content and brand keep paying off, a flywheel), while paid is a *pump* that stops the instant you stop spending - so a business overweight on paid has a structurally fragile growth engine. Scalability cuts the other way: paid scales fast and predictably (turn up budget), organic scales slowly. So the framework is to map each channel onto a 2x2 of unit economics (CAC:LTV) vs durability (loop vs pump), favor compounding channels for the base, and use paid for controllable, incremental growth on top - always validated with incrementality, since organic and direct are partly *caused* by paid.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios segments the funnel and revenue by `channel_group` and computes `revenue_per_session` per channel, giving the quality (not just volume) read for each; its honesty about lacking spend data keeps the paid/organic comparison to what the observational dataset can defend.
**Follow-ups.** How do you isolate the incremental value of organic that paid actually drove? / Which channel would you cut first in a downturn and why? / How does the loop-vs-pump distinction change a forecast?

#### Q7. What is an acquisition cohort and what questions does it answer that a cross-sectional view can't?
**Ideal answer.** An acquisition cohort groups users by *when* (and often *how*) they were acquired - e.g., "users first seen in week of 2020-12-07 via Paid Search" - and then tracks that fixed group's behavior forward over time. The questions it answers that a cross-sectional snapshot cannot are about *trajectory and quality drift*: is the December cohort activating and converting better or worse than the November cohort, and is a channel's customer *quality* degrading even while its volume grows? A cross-sectional "conversion rate this month" blends new and old users and hides the fact that, say, a scaled Paid Social channel is bringing progressively worse cohorts. Cohorting also separates acquisition effects from retention effects: a falling overall active-user count can be a *new-cohort acquisition* problem or an *old-cohort retention* problem, and only a cohort table (acquisition period on the rows, age on the columns) tells you which. The frequent mistake is comparing cohorts of different ages as if they were comparable - you must align on cohort *age*, not calendar date.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's `stats-mcp.cohort_retention` builds these triangles deterministically, and because identity is cookie-grain (`user_pseudo_id`, since `user_id` is almost always null), it is explicit that cohorts are cookie-based approximations - a stated assumption rather than a hidden one.
**Follow-ups.** How would you tell an acquisition problem from a retention problem in a declining MAU? / Why must you compare cohorts at equal age? / What does cookie-grain identity do to cohort sizes over time?

#### Q8. Define a growth loop and contrast it with a funnel. Give an acquisition-loop example.
**Ideal answer.** A funnel is a *linear, one-way* model - traffic enters at the top and a shrinking fraction reaches the bottom - whereas a growth loop is *circular*: the output of the loop feeds back as input to the next turn, so growth compounds instead of requiring fresh top-of-funnel spend each time. The classic acquisition loop is content/SEO: a user lands on a page, that engagement and any user-generated content strengthen the page's ranking, which ranks for more queries, which acquires more users - the output (users + content) becomes the input (better ranking). A referral loop is even cleaner: acquired users invite others who become acquired users. The strategic point is that loops have *defensible, compounding* economics while funnels are *leaky and need constant refilling*, so a great growth team instruments the loop's cycle time and amplification factor (how many new users each user generates), not just the funnel's step rates. Funnels and loops are complementary - you still optimize the funnel *inside* each turn of the loop.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios models the GA4 macro funnel (session_start -> view_item -> add_to_cart -> begin_checkout -> add_shipping_info -> add_payment_info -> purchase) as its diagnostic spine while framing channel mix in loop terms - it can flag when a compounding channel (Organic Search) is losing share to a pump channel, which is a loop-health signal a pure funnel view misses.
**Follow-ups.** What two metrics define a loop's health? / How would you instrument referral-loop amplification in GA4? / When is a funnel the right model and a loop the wrong one?

#### Q9. Acquisition volume is up but the overall funnel conversion rate dropped. Is the funnel broken? How do you prove it isn't (or is)?
**Ideal answer.** Not necessarily - this is the textbook mix-shift-masquerading-as-rate-change situation, and you prove which it is with a decomposition, not an eyeball. If acquisition grew by adding sessions from a low-converting channel or device, the *blended* `session_conversion_rate` falls purely because the weights `w_i` shifted toward low-`r_i` segments, even if every segment's own conversion rate is flat or improving (Simpson's paradox). To prove it, I decompose `Î”(conversion_rate)` into `mix = Î£ Î”w_iÂ·r_i(t0)` (composition moved), `rate = Î£ w_i(t0)Â·Î”r_i` (behavior moved), and `interaction`. If the mix term explains nearly all of the drop and per-segment rates are stable, the funnel is *not* broken - you just changed who's walking into it. If the rate term dominates, then a real behavioral/UX problem exists and I'd drill the worst-affected segment. The discipline is to *always drill rate effects before mix effects*, because rate effects are real behavior change you can fix, while mix effects are composition artifacts you manage at the acquisition layer.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** This is Helios's reason to exist: the Decompose agent calls `stats-mcp.decompose_change` to split the movement, and the Diagnose agent prioritizes rate effects over mix effects in its hypothesis tree - so "is the funnel broken?" gets a deterministic, dollar-quantified answer instead of a debate.
**Follow-ups.** What does a large interaction term imply about the two periods? / If mix dominates, what's the recommended action vs if rate dominates? / How do you guard the decomposition against a tiny-sample segment dominating the math?

#### Q10. How do you detect and quantify a mix-shift in acquisition channels specifically?
**Ideal answer.** A mix-shift in acquisition is a change in the *volume share* `w_i` of channels between two periods, independent of how each channel converts. I detect it by computing each `channel_group`'s session share in `t0` and `t1`, flagging channels whose share moved beyond a noise threshold, and then quantifying the *business impact* of that shift by holding each channel's own conversion/`revenue_per_session` fixed and asking: how much did blended revenue move purely because the weights changed? That isolated number is the mix effect, `Î£ Î”w_i Â· r_i(t0)`. Practically:
```sql
SELECT channel_group, period,
       SUM(sessions) / SUM(SUM(sessions)) OVER (PARTITION BY period) AS volume_share,
       SUM(revenue)  / SUM(sessions)                                 AS rev_per_session
FROM helios.marts.fct_daily_funnel
GROUP BY channel_group, period
```
Then the mix effect is the share deltas weighted by baseline `rev_per_session`. The key sophistication is reporting the mix-shift *in dollars*, not just "Display share rose 6 points," because a share shift toward a high-RPS channel is good news and toward a low-RPS channel is a quantifiable revenue drag - same direction of share change, opposite business meaning.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's offline benchmark literally injects *volume perturbations* (change `w_i` only, holding rates fixed) as labeled mix-shift scenarios, and grades whether the pipeline rediscovers them; `stats-mcp.decompose_change` returns the mix effect in the metric's units so it can be priced as revenue-at-risk.
**Follow-ups.** How big a share change is "signal" vs seasonal noise? / Why can a mix-shift toward a high-RPS channel still be a risk? / How would you separate a genuine acquisition mix-shift from a tracking/tagging change?

#### Q11. Where does acquisition sit in AARRR, and what's the single metric you'd watch for acquisition health?
**Ideal answer.** Acquisition is the first A in AARRR - the top of the pirate-metrics funnel - and it measures whether you can reliably bring qualified strangers to the product. The single metric depends on the business model, but for an e-commerce store I'd watch *qualified new sessions by channel* (or new-user sessions) as the volume pulse, paired with a quality guardrail like `session_conversion_rate` or `revenue_per_session` of the *newly acquired* traffic - because volume without quality is vanity. If forced to one composite, I'd lean toward CAC-payback or new-customer revenue contribution, since it bakes cost and quality together. The framework discipline is that acquisition should never be optimized in isolation: a North Star metric (e.g., weekly purchasing users, or revenue per active user) sits above AARRR and forces acquisition to be judged by the *quality* of users it feeds into Activation and Retention, not raw traffic. Optimizing acquisition volume against a North Star prevents the classic failure of celebrating a traffic spike that never converts.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios treats the acquisition layer as the *source of weights* `w_i` feeding the funnel and always pairs a volume read with `revenue_per_session` per `channel_group`, so acquisition is judged by downstream quality - the AARRR-into-North-Star linkage made concrete.
**Follow-ups.** Why is raw session volume a vanity metric? / How would you design a North Star that keeps acquisition honest? / What guardrail metric stops acquisition from gaming the North Star?

#### Q12. How would you prioritize which acquisition channel to invest in next? Use a scoring framework.
**Ideal answer.** I'd use a RICE or ICE scoring pass over candidate channel bets so prioritization is transparent and arguable on inputs, not vibes. ICE scores each option on Impact (expected revenue lift if it works), Confidence (how sure I am, grounded in cohort quality and prior data), and Ease (effort/cost to test), and I multiply or average them; RICE adds Reach (how many users the bet touches) and divides by Effort: `RICE = (Reach Ã— Impact Ã— Confidence) / Effort`. For acquisition specifically, Impact should be the *dollar* opportunity (e.g., the revenue-at-risk or revenue-upside a decomposition surfaced for that channel), Confidence should be pulled down for channels where the data is observational or the cohort is small, and Effort includes both creative/ops cost and the experiment runtime needed to read a result. The senior nuance is that the highest raw-ICE channel isn't always the pick - you weight toward *compounding* (loop) channels for long-run defensibility and reserve fast paid bets for filling near-term gaps. The output is a ranked, justifiable backlog, not a single hero bet.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's Prescribe agent emits a *prioritized, powered* experiment backlog - each card carries the dollar impact (from the decomposition), a confidence grounded in significance testing, and a runtime estimate from `experiment-mcp` (`power_analysis`/`runtime_estimate`) - which is exactly the Impact/Confidence/Effort triad RICE formalizes.
**Follow-ups.** When does the top-RICE item not get built? / How do you keep Confidence honest on observational data? / How does experiment runtime feed the Effort term?

#### Q13. How do seasonality and one-off campaigns confound acquisition trend analysis, and how do you control for them?
**Ideal answer.** Acquisition traffic is highly seasonal and event-driven - Black Friday, Cyber Monday, holiday gifting, a product launch, or a one-day email blast can swing channel volumes and mix dramatically - so a naive period-over-period comparison will mislabel a calendar effect as a real change. I control for this three ways: compare against the right baseline (year-over-year or the same weekday/week-of-season, not just last week), maintain a *known-events calendar* so a spike on a launch date isn't flagged as an anomaly, and use a forecast with prediction intervals (e.g., a seasonal model) so I'm comparing actuals against an expected band rather than a flat line. The decomposition then runs on the *residual* - the part of the movement the seasonal expectation didn't already explain. The failure mode to avoid is "seasonality theater," where every real regression gets dismissed as "probably just seasonal"; the discipline is to quantify the seasonal expectation explicitly and require the residual to clear a significance bar before raising an alarm.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's `stats-mcp.forecast` (Prophet/statsmodels) sets the expected band, its memory store holds a seasonality/launch calendar, and the Critic agent runs an explicit *seasonality* refutation that can DOWNGRADE or DROP a finding - and the offline benchmark includes deliberate "seasonality decoy" scenarios to ensure Helios doesn't fire on calendar effects.
**Follow-ups.** What baseline do you pick when there's no prior-year data? / How does a launch-calendar entry change anomaly scoring? / How do you avoid over-suppressing real regressions as "seasonal"?

### Activation

#### Q14. Define activation. How is it different from acquisition and from conversion?
**Ideal answer.** Activation is the second A in AARRR: the moment a newly acquired user first experiences the product's core value - the point at which they "get it" and become meaningfully more likely to stick. It's distinct from acquisition (which only got them in the door - a session_start) and from a one-time conversion (which is a transaction, a Revenue-stage event); activation is specifically about *first realized value*, which for a SaaS product might be "completed setup and ran a first report" and for e-commerce is closer to "found a relevant product and engaged with it" (a `view_item` or `add_to_cart`), with purchase being the eventual conversion downstream. The reason the distinction matters operationally is that the *fix* differs: low acquisition is a marketing problem, low activation is an onboarding/first-experience problem, and low conversion can be a pricing/checkout problem. Conflating them sends you optimizing the wrong stage. A precise activation definition is the prerequisite for an activation *rate*, which is the lever between traffic and retention.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's funnel separates the acquisition entry (`session_start`), the activation/engagement steps (`view_item`, `add_to_cart`), and the conversion steps (`begin_checkout` -> `purchase`) as distinct, separately-decomposable stages - so a drop can be localized to activation rather than blamed on acquisition or checkout.
**Follow-ups.** What's the e-commerce equivalent of a SaaS "aha moment"? / Why does conflating activation and conversion mislead the fix? / Where does engagement_rate fit relative to activation?

#### Q15. What is the "aha moment" and how would you find it empirically?
**Ideal answer.** The aha moment is the specific early action (or set of actions) that correlates most strongly with a user becoming a retained, valuable customer - Facebook's "7 friends in 10 days" and Slack's "2,000 messages" are the canonical examples. I find it empirically by taking an acquisition cohort, enumerating candidate early behaviors (e.g., viewed N items, added to cart, used search, returned within X days), and measuring which behavior best *separates* retained/converted users from churned ones - i.e., the action where the gap in downstream retention between users who did it and didn't is largest and the curve flattens (a threshold effect). I'm careful to frame this as *correlational* and then validate causality: the right test is whether *driving* more users to that action via onboarding actually lifts retention (a quasi-experiment or A/B), because otherwise I might just be describing what engaged users do anyway, not what *causes* engagement. The output is a crisp, measurable activation definition the whole team can rally around.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios is explicit that its dataset is observational, so it surfaces *candidate* leading indicators by decomposing the activation funnel and then has the Prescribe agent *design and size the experiment* to test whether driving that behavior is causal - it never claims correlation is causation, which is the exact discipline aha-moment analysis requires.
**Follow-ups.** How do you avoid mistaking a correlate of engagement for a cause? / What sample size do you need to trust the threshold? / How would you A/B test driving users to the aha action?

#### Q16. Define activation rate precisely and write how you'd compute it.
**Ideal answer.** Activation rate is the share of newly acquired users (or sessions) who reach the activation milestone within a defined window - precision requires nailing the numerator (the activation event), the denominator (which users, over what acquisition window), and the time bound (within N days of acquisition). For the GA4 store, a defensible session-grain definition is "sessions that reached the engagement/value step out of all sessions," which maps to a funnel-step rate:
```sql
SELECT
  channel_group,
  SUM(view_item_sessions)             AS activated_sessions,   -- reached value step
  SUM(sessions)                       AS sessions,
  SUM(view_item_sessions) / SUM(sessions) AS activation_rate
FROM helios.marts.fct_daily_funnel
GROUP BY channel_group
```
Two non-negotiables: compute the rate as `SUM(numerator)/SUM(denominator)` after grouping (never the average of per-segment ratios - that reintroduces Simpson's paradox), and use the monotonic "reached" definition so the numerator counts a session that reached the value step *or any later step*, keeping the rate bounded by 1. The exact milestone is a product choice; the rigor is in fixing the cohort and window so the rate is comparable over time.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's `int_ga4__funnel_steps` uses `reached_*` max-downstream monotonic flags (guaranteeing `sessions >= reached_view_item >= ... >= reached_purchase`, so every step rate is <= 1), and the semantic layer computes rates as `SUM(num)/SUM(den)` - so an activation-rate metric is governed and Simpson-safe by construction.
**Follow-ups.** Why use a monotonic "reached" flag instead of a per-step "did" flag? / How does the activation window length change the rate? / Session-grain vs user-grain activation - which and why here?

#### Q17. Walk me through how you'd map and diagnose an onboarding (activation) funnel.
**Ideal answer.** I map the onboarding funnel as the ordered sequence of steps from entry to first value, measure the *step-to-step* conversion at each, and look for the biggest drop-off step relative to a benchmark - that's where the leak is. For the store the activation segment of the funnel is `session_start -> view_item -> add_to_cart`, and I'd compute `view_to_cart_rate` and the step rates around it, then segment the worst step by device, channel, and landing page to localize the problem (e.g., the drop is concentrated on mobile, or on traffic from one channel landing on a slow page). Diagnosing *why* a step fell requires the mix-vs-rate split again: did the step rate genuinely drop (a real UX/onboarding problem) or did the *composition* of users hitting that step shift toward a segment that always converted worse? Only after isolating a real rate drop in a real segment do I form a hypothesis (e.g., a regression on the mobile product page) and prescribe a fix. The framework is: ordered steps -> biggest leak -> segment the leak -> decompose mix vs rate -> hypothesis -> sized experiment.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's Decompose agent runs on each funnel step (`view_to_cart_rate`, `cart_to_checkout_rate`, `checkout_to_purchase_rate`) and the Diagnose agent runs a best-first hypothesis-tree RCA that's SQL-verified at every node - so an onboarding-funnel leak is localized to a step *and* a segment *and* attributed to rate-not-mix, then handed to Prescribe.
**Follow-ups.** How do you pick the benchmark for a "good" step rate? / What if the leak is split across two steps? / How do you tell a real step regression from a composition shift at that step?

#### Q18. What is time-to-value (TTV) and why does it matter for activation?
**Ideal answer.** Time-to-value is the elapsed time from a user's first contact (acquisition) to the moment they first experience core value (activation) - shorter TTV means users hit the aha moment before they lose interest, so it's one of the strongest levers on activation rate and, downstream, on retention. It matters because attention decays fast: every extra step, form field, or minute before first value bleeds users, and reducing TTV often lifts activation more cheaply than buying more traffic. I'd measure it as the median (not mean, because it's right-skewed) time or step-count from `session_start` to the activation event, segment it (mobile TTV is usually worse), and treat a long TTV at a specific step as a prioritized onboarding fix. For e-commerce, TTV maps to "how fast can a visitor find a relevant product" - search quality, navigation, and page speed - so it ties directly to the early funnel steps. The framework link is that TTV is the *speed* dimension of activation, while activation rate is the *completion* dimension; you want both high-completion and low-time.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's sessionized data carries event timestamps, so the time and step distance from `session_start` to the value step (`view_item`/`add_to_cart`) is derivable and decomposable by segment - letting a TTV regression be localized (e.g., mobile) the same way a rate regression is.
**Follow-ups.** Why median over mean for TTV? / How would you trade off TTV against richness of the first experience? / How does TTV differ on mobile vs desktop and why?

#### Q19. What are leading indicators of retention, and how do they relate to activation?
**Ideal answer.** Leading indicators of retention are *early*, observable behaviors that predict whether a user will stick around long before the retention itself is measurable - they're the bridge between activation and the Retention stage of AARRR, and activation milestones are usually the strongest ones. Examples: reaching the aha action within the first session/week, returning within N days of first visit, breadth of early engagement (viewed multiple categories, used search), and for e-commerce, adding to cart or completing a first purchase. They matter because retention is a *lagging* metric - you can't wait 90 days to learn a cohort is bad - so you instrument leading indicators to get a same-week read on cohort quality and to know which onboarding changes are working. The analytical method is the same as finding the aha moment: regress/segment early behaviors against eventual retention, pick the ones with the strongest, most stable separation, and validate causally. The mature caveat is that a leading indicator must be validated as *predictive out-of-sample*, not just fit in-sample, or you'll chase a spurious early signal.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios is honest that its ~3-month window (Nov 2020-Jan 2021) defers true long-horizon retention/LTV, so it reports *short-horizon retention proxies with stated assumptions* and uses `stats-mcp.cohort_retention` to relate early activation behaviors to the near-term return signal - leading indicators done with explicit limitation-flagging.
**Follow-ups.** How do you validate a leading indicator is predictive, not just correlated? / What proxy would you use when you only have 90 days of data? / How would activation rate itself serve as a leading indicator?

#### Q20. Activation rate dropped this week. Walk me through your diagnosis before you alarm anyone.
**Ideal answer.** First I'd check data quality - a tracking break, a tag misfire, or a schema change can fake an activation drop, and shipping a false alarm burns credibility - so I reconcile the event volumes against canonical totals before trusting the number. Then I'd ask whether the drop clears the noise/seasonal band: compare against the right baseline and a forecast interval, not last week's point. If it's real, I decompose `Î”(activation_rate)` into mix vs rate: a shift in *who* arrived (e.g., a surge of low-intent Display traffic) can drop blended activation with every segment's own rate intact (mix), versus a genuine in-segment drop (rate) that signals an onboarding/UX regression. If rate dominates, I segment to localize it (device, channel, landing page), form a hypothesis, run a significance test to confirm the affected-segment drop isn't sampling noise, and only then quantify the dollar impact and raise it - with a recommended, sized experiment to fix it. The order is deliberate: data-quality -> seasonality -> mix-vs-rate -> segment -> significance -> dollars -> action.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** This is the Helios pipeline end to end: `warehouse-mcp.reconcile` (<=0.5% drift) guards data quality, `stats-mcp.forecast` sets the band, `decompose_change` splits mix/rate, `significance_test` confirms the segment, and the Critic adversarially attacks the finding (data-quality / seasonality / insufficient-sample / mix-confound) before the Narrator writes the Decision Brief.
**Follow-ups.** Why is data-quality the first check, not the decomposition? / What significance bar would you require on the affected segment? / How do you price an activation drop in dollars when activation isn't a purchase?

#### Q21. How would you design an experiment to improve activation, and how do you size it?
**Ideal answer.** I'd start from a diagnosis (which step leaks, in which segment, mix vs rate) so the experiment targets a real, sized opportunity, then write a hypothesis with one primary metric (the activation rate at the leaking step) and a guardrail (e.g., downstream purchase rate, so I don't lift activation at the cost of conversion). Sizing is a power-analysis problem: given the baseline rate, the minimum detectable effect (MDE) worth shipping, and target power (0.8) and significance (0.05), I compute the required sample per arm and then translate it into runtime given the segment's daily traffic - a low-traffic segment may need weeks, which itself is a prioritization input. I'd guard against the classic mistakes: peeking (fixing the horizon up front or using sequential methods), under-powering (calling a flat result a "no effect" when the test couldn't detect the MDE), and ignoring the guardrail. If a live A/B isn't possible, I design a quasi-experiment - a pre/post or difference-in-differences readback against a comparable unaffected segment as the control.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's `experiment-mcp` does exactly this - `power_analysis` -> `runtime_estimate` -> `design_experiment` - and because the GA4 data is observational, Prescribe emits quasi-experimental (pre/post, diff-in-diff) readback designs, with each card carrying the dollar impact and runtime that feed the RICE-style backlog ranking.
**Follow-ups.** What guardrail metric stops you gaming activation? / How does low segment traffic change your design? / Why is peeking at the result dangerous and how do you prevent it?

#### Q22. How does the mix-shift-vs-rate-change distinction apply inside the activation funnel, not just acquisition?
**Ideal answer.** It applies at *every* rate in the funnel, including each activation step, because any aggregate step rate is `R = Î£ w_iÂ·r_i` over segments - so a change in `view_to_cart_rate` can be driven by a shift in *who* reached `view_item` (mix) or by a genuine change in how each segment behaves at that step (rate), exactly as at the acquisition layer. The practical consequence is that an "activation is dropping" alarm must be decomposed before it's actioned: if mobile traffic share rose and mobile always carries a lower view-to-cart rate, blended activation falls with no real UX regression (mix), and the lever is upstream acquisition targeting, not the product page. If instead the desktop view-to-cart rate itself fell, that's a real activation regression (rate) and the lever is the onboarding/page experience. Same number, opposite owner and opposite fix - which is precisely why you never act on a blended funnel rate without splitting it. This is also how you dissolve Simpson's paradox *inside* activation, where overall activation can drop while every segment improves.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's Decompose agent runs `decompose_change` on the funnel-step rates (`view_to_cart_rate`, `cart_to_checkout_rate`, etc.), not just on acquisition volume - so the mix/rate/interaction identity is applied throughout activation, and the offline benchmark's rate vs volume perturbations test both layers.
**Follow-ups.** Give a Simpson's-paradox example inside activation. / How do you decide which segmentation dimension to decompose by? / If interaction is large at a step, what does that say?

#### Q23. What activation metric would you nominate as (or feed into) a North Star, and why?
**Ideal answer.** A North Star metric should capture *delivered customer value* and predict long-term growth, so for an e-commerce store I'd nominate something like *weekly purchasing users* (or revenue per active user), and the *activation* metric that feeds it is "share of new users who reach first value within their first session/week." The logic is the AARRR chain: activation is the leading lever that converts acquired traffic into the retained, revenue-generating users the North Star counts, so an activation milestone is a high-quality *input* metric to the North Star rather than the North Star itself. I'd avoid making raw activation events the North Star, because they can be gamed (lower the value bar and "activation" rises while real value doesn't) - which is why the North Star sits above AARRR and forces every stage, including activation, to be judged by downstream value. The test of a good North Star is that moving it reliably moves revenue and retention; activation rate earns its place because it demonstrably leads both.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's metric registry (28 metrics, including `revenue_per_user`/ARPU and `revenue_per_session`) lets activation milestones be evaluated against downstream value metrics in the same governed query - so the activation-to-North-Star linkage is computed on one source of truth, not stitched across tools.
**Follow-ups.** Why not make the activation event itself the North Star? / How would you detect if activation is being gamed? / What guardrails sit beside a purchasing-users North Star?

#### Q24. How do you connect activation back to acquisition channel quality?
**Ideal answer.** Activation is the cleanest *quality* signal for an acquisition channel, because it measures whether the users a channel brings actually engage with core value, not just whether they showed up - so I cross-tabulate activation rate by `channel_group` (and campaign) on the *new-user* cohort and compare. A channel with high session volume but low activation rate is bringing low-intent traffic, and that's a budget-reallocation signal even before you get to purchase - it tells you the problem is upstream targeting, not the product. This closes the AARRR loop between the two stages: you judge acquisition not by volume but by the activation (and eventual retention) of the cohorts it delivers, which is exactly how you avoid scaling a channel that pumps in traffic that never activates. The analytical care is to compare channels at equal cohort age and to decompose so you don't confuse a channel's intrinsic quality with a temporary mix-shift in *which* of its sub-segments arrived.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Because Helios pivots both the activation funnel steps and `revenue_per_session` by `channel_group`, it can directly attribute a low activation rate to a specific channel and price the reallocation opportunity in dollars - making "this channel's traffic doesn't activate" a quantified finding rather than an assertion.
**Follow-ups.** What does a high-volume/low-activation channel tell you to do? / How do you separate channel quality from a within-channel mix-shift? / Why compare activation at equal cohort age across channels?

#### Q25. Tie it together: how do AARRR, growth loops, and the mix-vs-rate lens combine into one operating model for diagnosing growth?
**Ideal answer.** AARRR gives the *map* - five stages (Acquisition, Activation, Retention, Referral, Revenue) where growth can be won or lost - and the first diagnostic move is always to localize a movement to a stage so you fix the right thing. Growth loops give the *engine* view layered on top: which stages feed back into each other (referral and content loops turn Revenue/Activation back into Acquisition), so you optimize for compounding, not just plugging leaks in a linear funnel. The mix-vs-rate lens is the *microscope* applied at every stage and every rate: any aggregate metric is `Î£ w_iÂ·r_i`, so before acting you decompose each movement into composition change (mix), behavior change (rate), and interaction - because they have different owners and opposite fixes, and conflating them (Simpson's paradox) is the single most common analytics error. Put together: AARRR tells you *where*, the decomposition tells you *why* (mix or rate), significance tells you *whether it's real*, dollarization tells you *how much it matters*, and a powered experiment tells you *what to do* - and the loop view keeps you investing in compounding stages. That sequence is a defensible operating model, not a dashboard.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios is this operating model instantiated end to end: Monitor localizes the movement (AARRR stage), Decompose runs the mix/rate identity, the Critic refutes it, Prescribe sizes the experiment, and the Narrator ships a dollar-quantified Decision Brief - grounded in governed SQL (semantic-mcp), deterministic math (stats-mcp), and graded by an 85%-vs-45% offline benchmark.
**Follow-ups.** Where would you start a diagnosis if you could only look at one AARRR stage? / How does the loop view change which leak you fix first? / What makes this an operating model rather than just reporting?

---

### Retention

#### Q26. Why do experienced growth people say retention is the most important part of the AARRR funnel, not acquisition?
**Ideal answer.** Acquisition is a leaky-bucket problem: if retention is poor, every dollar of paid acquisition pours into a bucket that drains, so you scale spend to stand still and CAC payback never closes. Retention is the only stage that compounds - it simultaneously lifts LTV (you monetize a user over more periods), funds acquisition (a higher LTV/CAC ratio lets you bid more aggressively than competitors), and feeds Referral (retained users are the only ones around long enough to invite others). I treat retention as the multiplier on all four other AARRR stages rather than a co-equal stage, which is why the North Star for most subscription and marketplace businesses is a retained-usage metric (WAU, retained GMV), not a top-of-funnel count. The honest nuance is that for a low-frequency transactional business, "retention" reads as repeat-purchase rate and reactivation, not daily engagement, so the framing changes but the compounding logic does not.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios models retention via `stats-mcp.cohort_retention` and `rfm_segment`, treating repeat-purchase/return-visit decay as a first-class diagnosable surface rather than a vanity acquisition count, consistent with the leaky-bucket view.
**Follow-ups.** When would acquisition legitimately outrank retention as the priority? / How does a strong LTV/CAC ratio mechanically let you out-bid competitors on acquisition? / What North Star would you pick for a low-frequency e-commerce store specifically?

#### Q27. Define a retention curve and explain what its shape tells you about product-market fit.
**Ideal answer.** A retention curve plots the fraction of a cohort still active in period N against N (D1, D7, D30, or week/month buckets), anchored to a defined "active" event. The diagnostic signal is whether the curve **flattens** to a non-zero asymptote or **decays to zero**: a flat tail means you have a stable, habitual core of users who will never churn for organic reasons, which is the empirical fingerprint of product-market fit; a curve that asymptotes at zero means you have no durable base and are running pure acquisition arbitrage. I read three numbers: the D1/W1 drop (onboarding and first-value delivery), the slope of the middle (the habit-forming zone), and the height of the flattened tail (the size of your retained core). The classic mistake is comparing only headline retention rates between cohorts without aligning the "active" definition and the cohort-entry event, which makes two curves non-comparable.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** `stats-mcp.cohort_retention` computes period-N survival per cohort deterministically, so the curve shape (and any flattening) is a tool output, never an eyeballed estimate, satisfying the determinism-where-it-matters principle.
**Follow-ups.** How do you decide the "active" event for a transactional store vs a SaaS app? / What does a smiling (upward-sloping tail) retention curve indicate and how is it possible? / How would you statistically test whether two cohorts' tails differ?

#### Q28. Walk me through the leaky-bucket framing and how you'd quantify the leak.
**Ideal answer.** The leaky bucket models your active base as a tank: acquisition is the inflow, churn is the outflow through holes, and the water level is your active users. The level grows only when inflow exceeds outflow, so the first thing I quantify is the **net retention dynamic**: new + resurrected - churned per period. I size the leak as `churned_users / active_users_start_of_period` and immediately decompose it - new-user churn (a first-value/onboarding hole) behaves differently from established-user churn (a value-erosion hole), and they get different fixes. The framing forces the right prioritization question: a 5-point reduction in churn is usually cheaper and more compounding than the acquisition spend required to offset the same leak, because plugging the hole improves every future cohort, not just this one.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's funnel marts and `cohort_retention` let it attribute a level-change to inflow vs outflow per segment, and `decompose_change` separates whether a retention dip is a mix artifact (more low-retention traffic acquired) or a true rate leak (existing users churning faster).
**Follow-ups.** How would you distinguish a leak caused by worse acquisition quality from one caused by product degradation? / What's the formula for the equilibrium active-user level given constant inflow and a constant churn rate? / Why might plugging the leak still not grow the base?

#### Q29. How would you build a churn-prediction model, and what's the trap most people fall into?
**Ideal answer.** I'd frame it as supervised classification: define churn precisely (e.g. no purchase in the next 90 days), build a feature set from RFM (recency, frequency, monetary), engagement trajectory, and tenure, then train a calibrated model (logistic regression or gradient-boosted trees) optimized for ranking quality - PR-AUC over accuracy, because churn is class-imbalanced. The pervasive trap is **target leakage**: including features that are mechanically downstream of churn (e.g. "days since last activity" measured into the prediction window) so the model "predicts" the outcome it's secretly observing - it scores beautifully offline and is useless in production. The second trap is optimizing AUC instead of **intervenable** churn: a model that flags users who were always going to leave has no business value; you want to surface the persuadable segment where an intervention changes the outcome, which is an uplift-modeling problem, not a pure-prediction one.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios stays deliberately on the diagnostic side: `rfm_segment` produces interpretable, intervenable segments (e.g. "at-risk high-value: high monetary, decaying recency") that map directly to a prescribed action, rather than a black-box churn score with no lever attached - reflecting principle 4, every finding carries an action.
**Follow-ups.** What's the difference between a propensity-to-churn model and an uplift model? / How do you set the prediction-window length without leaking the label? / Why might a high-AUC churn model still lose money in production?

#### Q30. What is resurrection (reactivation) and how do you measure whether a resurrection program is actually working?
**Ideal answer.** Resurrection is bringing a churned/dormant user back to active status; in AARRR accounting it's a third inflow alongside new acquisition, and it's usually the cheapest growth dollar because the user already knows the product. I measure it as a cohort: take everyone dormant as of date T, apply the intervention, and track the **resurrection rate** = reactivated / dormant-targeted, but the number that matters is **second-life retention** - do resurrected users stick, or do they bounce back to dormant within a period? A program that resurrects 10% who all re-churn in 30 days is theatre. The correct evaluation is quasi-experimental: a holdout of dormant users who get no intervention, so you measure incremental resurrection (organic dormant users do reactivate on their own), and you net out that baseline before claiming credit.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's `rfm_segment` isolates the "lapsed" cells, and because the GA4 data is observational, Helios's `experiment-mcp` would **design and size** a resurrection holdout (power_analysis + runtime_estimate) and read it back as a pre/post difference-in-differences rather than asserting a raw before/after lift.
**Follow-ups.** Why is a dormant-user holdout essential rather than a simple before/after? / How do you set the dormancy threshold that defines "resurrectable"? / What's the risk of over-messaging dormant users to resurrect them?

#### Q31. Distinguish retention, churn, and engagement - candidates conflate these constantly.
**Ideal answer.** Retention and churn are complements measured on a cohort over time - retention is the fraction still active in period N, churn is `1 - retention` for that period, and both require a fixed cohort and an "active" definition. Engagement is an **intensity** measure within the active population - sessions per user, items viewed, DAU/MAU stickiness ratio - and a user can be retained but disengaged (logs in but does little) or highly engaged right before churning (a usage spike can precede cancellation). The practical consequence is that engagement is a **leading indicator** of future retention but is not retention itself; I use engagement trends to forecast where the retention curve is heading and to find at-risk users before the churn event fires, but I never report rising engagement as if it were proof of improved retention.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios tracks `engaged_sessions` and `engagement_rate` as distinct metrics from session/return-visit retention in the semantic registry, keeping intensity and survival separate so the Diagnose agent can flag "engagement falling ahead of retention" as a leading-indicator finding.
**Follow-ups.** Give an example where engagement rose but retention fell - what's the explanation? / Why is DAU/MAU a stickiness measure and not a retention measure? / How would you use an engagement leading indicator to trigger an intervention?

#### Q32. How do you choose retention period buckets (D1/D7/D30 vs W1/W4/M3) for a given product?
**Ideal answer.** The bucketing must match the product's **natural usage frequency** - the cadence at which a satisfied user would naturally return. A daily-habit product (social, news) uses D1/D7/D30 because a day is a meaningful unit; a weekly product (grocery, fitness) uses week buckets; a low-frequency transactional store like the GA4 Merch Store uses month or even quarter buckets because expecting a D1 return is nonsensical and would manufacture an artificially brutal curve. The test I apply: pick the bucket where the median active user's inter-event gap fits comfortably inside one bucket, so a single skipped period isn't immediately misread as churn. Getting this wrong is a silent error - too-tight buckets make a healthy low-frequency product look like it's hemorrhaging, and too-loose buckets hide a real daily-product problem until it's catastrophic.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** On the GA4 Merch Store's transactional, low-frequency pattern Helios uses return-visit / repeat-purchase windows appropriate to that cadence rather than DAU-style daily retention, and is explicit (a maturity point) that the ~3-month dataset window caps how long a bucket horizon it can honestly report.
**Follow-ups.** What inter-event-gap distribution would justify switching from weekly to monthly buckets? / How does the 3-month GA4 window constrain your bucket choice? / How do you handle a product with two distinct usage frequencies (power users vs casual)?

#### Q33. A cohort's retention looks flat overall but is collapsing in one segment. How does Simpson's paradox apply, and how do you catch it?
**Ideal answer.** Aggregate retention `R = Î£ w_i Â· r_i` is a volume-weighted blend of per-segment retention rates, so a severe collapse in one segment can be masked at the top line by a simultaneous **mix shift** toward a higher-retaining segment - the classic Simpson's-paradox structure. To catch it I never trust the aggregate alone; I decompose the change into a **mix effect** (`Î£ Î”w_i Â· r_i`, composition moved) and a **rate effect** (`Î£ w_i Â· Î”r_i`, in-segment behavior moved), and I drill the rate effects because those are the real behavior change. A flat aggregate with a large negative rate effect in one segment offset by a positive mix effect is exactly the failure that a dashboard's single retention number hides, and it's the situation that costs analysts a day of manual slicing.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** This is the literal centerpiece: `stats-mcp.decompose_change` splits `Î”R` into mix/rate/interaction so a hidden segment collapse surfaces as a rate effect even when the top-line is flat - the exact Simpson's-paradox dissolution Helios was built around.
**Follow-ups.** Write the three decomposition terms and state which one you'd drill first. / How do you guard against computing the aggregate rate as a mean of per-segment ratios? / What does a large interaction term tell you mechanically?

#### Q34. How would you set a retention target and tie it to a North Star metric without gaming it?
**Ideal answer.** I anchor retention to a North Star that captures **delivered value**, not activity - retained-usage or retained-revenue, something a user would only do if they got real value (e.g. "monthly active purchasers" rather than "monthly logins"). The target is set off the retention curve's flattening point: I commit to lifting the **asymptotic tail** (the durable core) by N points, not just the easily-gamed D1 number, because early-period retention can be juiced with notification spam that erodes the tail. To prevent gaming I pair the headline target with a guardrail metric - e.g. unsubscribe/complaint rate or per-user value - so a "win" that's actually annoyance-driven is caught. Frameworks: North Star + input metrics tree, and I prioritize the candidate retention levers with ICE/RICE so effort goes to high-confidence, high-reach interventions first.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's Prescribe agent emits **ICE/RICE-scored, powered** experiment cards for retention levers and the Critic refutes findings that would game a metric (insufficient sample, seasonality, data-quality), so a target is only "moved" by a defensible, guardrail-aware intervention.
**Follow-ups.** What guardrail metric pairs with a D1-retention target? / Why is the tail height a better target than the D1 drop? / How would RICE rank a notification change vs an onboarding redesign?

### Referral

#### Q35. Define the K-factor (viral coefficient) and explain what value range actually matters in practice.
