**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios declares its seven agents and the Decision Brief as exposures in `exposures.yml`, so `dbt ls --select +exposure:helios_decision_brief` answers "what feeds the brief?" and a change can be impact-analyzed all the way to the consuming agent before it ships.
**Follow-ups.** What metadata should an exposure carry to be useful (owner, maturity, URL)? / How do exposures change your CI selection logic? / Can an exposure depend on the semantic layer rather than a raw mart, and why prefer that?

#### Q45. How do you perform impact analysis before shipping a model change?
**Ideal answer.** Impact analysis answers "what does this change touch?" before it touches it. I use the lineage graph to select the changed model plus its full downstream closure, including exposures, and build/test exactly that subgraph - so I see every affected mart, metric, and consumer rather than guessing. The pattern is `+changed_model+` to get upstream context and all descendants, intersected with `exposure:*` to surface business-facing impact. This converts a risky "I think this only affects X" into a verified blast radius, and it lets reviewers reason about scope. The discipline matters most for shared upstream models like sessionization, where a one-line change can ripple to every fact and every agent.
```bash
dbt ls    --select +int_ga4__sessionized+      # full blast radius
dbt build --select int_ga4__sessionized+ exposure:*   # build descendants + consumers
```
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios uses `dbt build --select +<changed_model>+exposure:*` so a change to a keystone like `int_ga4__sessionized` shows everything it touches - every mart and every agent exposure - before merge, backed by the CI eval gate as the final blast-radius check.
**Follow-ups.** Why include exposures in the selection rather than just models? / How does impact analysis interact with slim CI's `state:modified+`? / What change would have the largest blast radius in your DAG, and how would you de-risk it?

#### Q46. How does lineage support data governance and access control?
**Ideal answer.** Governance is about controlling *what* logic is authoritative and *who* can reach which data, and lineage is the backbone of both. Lineage lets you prove provenance (this number derives from these governed models, not an ad-hoc query), enforce that sensitive sources only flow through approved transforms, and scope credentials by layer so a consumer physically cannot bypass governance to hit raw data. The strongest control is structural: grant the consuming service read access only to the governed layer, so even a compromised or misbehaving consumer cannot reach raw facts. Lineage makes that boundary auditable - you can see, and test, that nothing reaches a consumer except through the governed path.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios backs `semantic-mcp` with a read-only service account scoped to the `helios_semantic` dataset only, so the LLM *physically cannot* query raw or mart tables - the governance boundary is enforced by IAM along the lineage, not just by policy.
**Follow-ups.** Why enforce the boundary with IAM rather than just instructing the agent not to query raw data? / How would you audit that no consumer bypasses the governed layer? / Where do column-level access controls (PII) fit into this?

#### Q47. How do you version and evolve data models and metric definitions without breaking consumers?
**Ideal answer.** I treat models and metric definitions as a versioned contract: changes go through PRs with impact analysis, breaking changes are flagged explicitly, and consumers reference *named, governed* metrics rather than raw SQL so a definition can evolve in one place. For genuinely breaking changes I use additive evolution where possible (introduce the new definition alongside the old, migrate consumers, then retire) and dbt's model contracts/versions to make a schema a promise CI enforces. The principle is that the cost of a change should be borne at author time through review and tests, not at consume time through a silent break. Version control plus the DAG means every definition has history and every change has a reviewable blast radius.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios centralizes every metric in `semantic_layer.yaml` and channel logic in one macro, so a definition changes in exactly one governed place and CI's eval gate catches any consumer-visible regression; the retirement of the non-monotonic `did_*` flags in favor of `reached_*` is a documented, repo-tracked breaking-change migration.
**Follow-ups.** How do dbt model contracts turn a schema into an enforced promise? / Walk through deprecating a metric with live consumers. / How does the eval baseline detect an unintended semantic drift from a "safe" definition change?

#### Q48. Explain "the registry as a contract." What does that buy you?
**Ideal answer.** A metric/dimension registry-as-contract means there is exactly one machine-readable definition of every metric and dimension, referentially compiled, that all SQL must be composed from - so the registry is not documentation, it is the enforced interface between intent and execution. It buys three things: a single source of truth (a metric is defined once, so two reports cannot disagree on what "conversion rate" means), structural elimination of invented columns (only registered names compile; an unknown name is a hard error, not free-text SQL), and safe evolution (change the contract, and every consumer follows). The tradeoff is upfront rigor - you must register a metric before you can query it - which is exactly the friction that prevents the sprawl of ad-hoc, subtly-divergent definitions.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's `semantic_layer.yaml` (47 metrics + 19 dimensions, referential-integrity compiled, 1:1 with dbt MetricFlow) is the sole SQL author via `semantic-mcp.build_query`; an unrecognized name is a hard error, which is how Helios achieves 0 hallucinated columns structurally rather than by hoping the LLM behaves.
**Follow-ups.** How is registry-as-contract different from just having a metrics layer? / Why does "unknown name is a hard error" matter more than a warning? / How does the registry map to dbt MetricFlow, and why align them 1:1?

#### Q49. How is the CI eval gate a "regression firewall," and what would happen without it?
**Ideal answer.** A regression firewall is an automated barrier that prevents a known-good capability from silently degrading - it makes correctness a *merge condition*, not an aspiration. The eval gate runs the labeled benchmark on every PR and blocks the merge if accuracy regresses beyond tolerance or any governance invariant breaks, so a refactor that "looks fine" but quietly drops root-cause accuracy can never reach `main`. Without it, accuracy erodes the way untested behavior always does: each change is individually plausible, no one re-runs the full benchmark by hand, and three months later the system is meaningfully worse with no single commit to blame. The firewall converts that slow, invisible decay into a loud, attributable red build on the exact PR that caused it.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's CI eval gate (`.github/workflows/ci.yml` + `eval/gates.yaml`) is explicitly the regression firewall: once green, no change may drop top-1 accuracy >2 points or introduce any hallucination - the 85%-vs-45% result is defended automatically on every commit, not just claimed once.
**Follow-ups.** Why is "every PR" essential - what breaks if it runs only nightly? / How do you keep the firewall itself trustworthy (i.e. test the tests)? / What's the failure mode if the baseline is updated carelessly?

#### Q50. Tie it together: how do data quality, testing, and lineage combine into a single trust story for an autonomous system?
**Ideal answer.** For an autonomous system that acts without a human in the loop, trust cannot be asserted - it has to be engineered into the pipeline as enforceable structure. The three layers compose: lineage defines and constrains the *paths* data can take (governed layer only, auditable provenance, impact-analyzable change); testing proves the *logic* on those paths is correct (golden tests on the centerpiece math, reconciliation against source, monotonicity invariants); and data-quality controls guard the *inputs and outputs* at runtime (freshness, reconciliation drift, the data-quality refutation). Each layer catches a different failure class, and the CI eval gate ties them together by proving the assembled system still produces the right end-to-end answer. The thesis is that the hard part of an AI analyst is not generating insight - it is making insight *correct and trusted*, and that is an analytics-engineering problem before it is a modeling one.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's three defensibility pillars are exactly this stack - governed-SQL grounding (lineage + registry), deterministic statistics (golden-tested tools), and adversarial verification plus the offline eval (the Critic + the regression-firewall benchmark) - which is why Helios is positioned as an engineered trust system, not a clever prompt.
**Follow-ups.** If you could keep only one of the three layers, which and why? / How would this trust stack change if Helios ran on live, multi-tenant data? / Where is the weakest link in this story today, and how would you harden it?

---

## Section 7: AI Engineering Interview

This section covers the AI-engineering substrate of Helios: the Model Context Protocol that turns trust principles into wiring, the tool-calling contract that makes governed SQL and deterministic math enforceable rather than aspirational, and the multi-agent orchestration that runs the diagnosis loop on a deterministic state machine. The throughline is a single thesis â€” the hard part of an AI analyst is not generating insights, it is making them correct and trusted â€” and every answer ties an industry-general concept back to a specific Helios server, agent, or guardrail.

### MCP

#### Q1. What is the Model Context Protocol, and what problem does it actually solve?
**Ideal answer.** MCP is an open client-server protocol that lets an LLM-driven agent invoke external capabilities ("tools"), read external state ("resources"), and reuse parameterized instruction templates ("prompts") over a single schema-typed JSON-RPC 2.0 interface. A server advertises a manifest of tools, each with a JSON Schema for its inputs and outputs; the client (the model runtime) marshals the model's structured tool calls to the server and returns typed results into context. The problem it solves is integration sprawl and trust: instead of every agent hand-rolling bespoke glue to each system, you get a uniform, introspectable, versionable capability surface â€” and because the surface is the *only* way the model touches the outside world, you can make that surface a hard boundary. The win is not "the model can call functions"; the win is that capabilities become declarative, discoverable, and governable.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios exposes exactly five MCP servers (warehouse, semantic, stats, experiment, report) and the Claude Agent SDK acts as the MCP client, loading them from `mcp_servers.yaml`, fetching each manifest, and exposing the union of tools to the agents â€” so every database access and every number is a typed tool call, never free text.
**Follow-ups.** How does a JSON-RPC tool manifest differ from an OpenAPI spec for the same backend? / What does MCP give you that a hand-written tool registry inside one process does not? / How do resources and prompts differ from tools, and did you use them in Helios?

#### Q2. Why adopt a tool *protocol* at all instead of just importing Python functions the agent can call?
**Ideal answer.** A protocol buys you process isolation, a stable typed contract, and independent deployment/versioning that in-process function imports cannot. If tools are just Python functions in the agent's address space, then the agent process holds every credential and every capability those functions need â€” a buggy or jailbroken agent can reach anything importable. A protocol lets each capability live behind a boundary with exactly its own credentials and blast radius, lets you swap a server's implementation without touching the agent, and gives you a single choke point to log, rate-limit, and validate every call. The cost is serialization overhead and an extra hop, which is real but small relative to LLM latency, and well worth it when the capability is "execute SQL against a billed warehouse."
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Only `warehouse-mcp` holds a BigQuery client and the service-account credential; the agent process and the four stdio servers inherit no warehouse credentials, so a compromised Narrator literally cannot reach BigQuery â€” a guarantee in-process imports cannot make.
**Follow-ups.** Where is the protocol overhead actually paid, and is it ever the bottleneck in a <5-min run? / How would you version a breaking change to `build_query`'s schema without a flag-day? / When would in-process tools genuinely be the right call?

#### Q3. stdio versus HTTP transport â€” how did you decide per server, and what are the tradeoffs?
**Ideal answer.** stdio runs the server as a child subprocess with JSON-RPC framed over stdin/stdout: lowest latency, no network surface, trivially co-located, but single-host and lifecycle-coupled to the parent. Streamable HTTP runs the server as a network service: it can live inside a network/security boundary, hold shared credentials, be reached by multiple clients, and scale independently â€” at the cost of a network surface that must be authenticated and a deployment to operate. The rule I apply: pure-compute, data-free, no-credential servers go on stdio; anything holding a sensitive credential or shared state that benefits from a security perimeter goes on HTTP. Transport is a deployment decision, not a capability one â€” the tool contract is identical either way.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** `semantic`, `stats`, `experiment`, and `report` run on stdio (pure compute, no warehouse credential), while `warehouse-mcp` runs over streamable HTTP precisely so it can sit in a network boundary with the BigQuery service account, be guarded by a bearer token (`HELIOS_WH_TOKEN`), and be shared across runs.
**Follow-ups.** What would force you to move `stats-mcp` off stdio to HTTP? / How do you authenticate the HTTP transport, and what's the threat model? / How does transport choice interact with horizontal scaling of runs?

#### Q4. Why split capability across *five* servers instead of one big tool server?
**Ideal answer.** The split follows the trust boundaries, not arbitrary modularity. The single most important separation is SQL *generation* (semantic) from SQL *execution* (warehouse), so a query is validated and reconciled before a byte is scanned and so the model cannot smuggle hand-written SQL into the executor â€” they are different servers with different privileges. Math (stats) is separated from data fetch so statistical methods are version-pinned and unit-tested independently of warehouse state. Experiment design and narration are isolated because they have zero data-access needs â€” they operate only on findings already produced. Each boundary is also a least-privilege and a blast-radius boundary: a bug or compromise is bounded by that server's tool catalog. The cost is more processes to operate; the payoff is that the trust story is *structural*.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The five-server split lets the guardrail chain be physically real: `semantic-mcp` emits SQL strings but cannot execute them, `report-mcp` writes briefs but cannot query the warehouse, and only `warehouse-mcp` touches BigQuery â€” so "the model can't hand-author SQL" is enforced by which server holds which capability, not by a prompt.
**Follow-ups.** Where would you draw the line if you had to collapse to three servers? / Doesn't five servers multiply operational overhead â€” how do you justify it for a portfolio-scale system? / Could one server's compromise still cascade, and how do you contain it?

#### Q5. Explain least privilege in this system â€” concretely, what can each server *not* do?
**Ideal answer.** Least privilege means every component holds exactly the credentials and scope it needs and nothing more, so the worst-case damage of a bug or compromise is bounded. In practice it shows up at two layers: per-server credential scoping and per-agent tool allow-lists. At the server layer, only the warehouse server has a BigQuery client, scoped to `bigquery.dataViewer` + `bigquery.jobUser`, read-only on the public dataset â€” no write, no delete, no other project. At the agent layer, each agent sees only the tools its role requires, so capability is sliced twice. The principle converts "please behave" into "you have no tool with which to misbehave."
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The per-agent allow-list is explicit: the Narrator is granted only `render_brief`, `save_diagnosis`, `export`, and `get_metric` â€” it has no `run_query` and no path to the warehouse â€” while the Decompose agent gets `build_query`, `run_query`, and `decompose_change` and nothing else, so even a misaligned agent's reach is bounded by its catalog.
**Follow-ups.** Why scope at the agent layer when the server layer already isolates credentials? / What's the minimum privilege the Critic needs, and why does it differ from Diagnose? / How would you audit that the allow-lists match the actual call graph in production?

#### Q6. How is MCP different from "raw" function/tool calling, and when does that difference matter?
**Ideal answer.** Raw function calling is the model-side mechanic â€” the model emits a structured call against a schema you hand it inline, and your application code dispatches it. MCP is the system-side mechanic â€” a standardized way to *host, discover, isolate, and govern* the things being called across process and trust boundaries. They are complementary layers, not competitors: under the hood, an MCP client still surfaces tools to the model via the same structured tool-calling interface. The difference matters the moment tools need different credentials, independent deployment, cross-agent reuse, or a hard enforcement boundary â€” exactly when "a function in my process" stops being safe enough. For a single-agent script with no privilege separation, raw tool calling is fine and MCP is overhead.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios needs the difference: the same tools must be reused by seven agents with *different* allow-lists, the warehouse credential must be isolated from the agent process, and the SQL-generation/execution boundary must be enforced â€” none of which falls out of raw in-process function calling, all of which MCP makes structural.
**Follow-ups.** If the SDK ultimately surfaces MCP tools as ordinary tool calls to the model, where exactly is the enforcement happening? / When is MCP overkill? / How does tool discovery via manifest help when the toolset evolves?

#### Q7. How does MCP make the grounding boundary *enforceable* rather than a prompt-time suggestion?
**Ideal answer.** A prompt that says "never write raw SQL" is advisory â€” a model can ignore it, and you only catch the violation after the fact, if at all. MCP makes it physical: you simply do not give the model a tool that executes arbitrary SQL. The only SQL-producing tool, `build_query`, interpolates *only* registry-defined metric templates and dimension expressions, so a hallucinated column like `event_params.foo` cannot survive composition â€” there is no template that references it, and unknown names raise a hard error. The model's reachable action space is the union of its allowed tools, so "can't" is a property of the wiring, not the wording. That is the difference between a policy and an invariant.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** `semantic-mcp` is the only path to SQL and `stats-mcp` is the only path to math; combined with `run_query` refusing any SQL not produced through that chain, Helios reaches its "0 hallucinated columns / 100% governed SQL" target by construction rather than by convention.
**Follow-ups.** Could the model still hallucinate a *metric name* â€” and what happens when it does? / Where would a determined model find a gap in this boundary? / How do you prove to an auditor that 100% of executed SQL was governed?

#### Q8. What are MCP resources and prompts, and why did Helios lean on tools instead?
**Ideal answer.** Beyond tools, MCP defines *resources* (read-only addressable state the client can pull into context, like a file or a row set) and *prompts* (reusable parameterized instruction templates a server offers). Tools are for actions with side effects or computation; resources are for context the model should *read*; prompts standardize recurring instruction patterns. Helios is overwhelmingly action-oriented â€” compose a query, run it under budget, decompose, test significance, render â€” so tools dominate. Read-only context that could be a resource (the metric/dimension catalog, prior diagnoses) is instead surfaced through explicit tools (`list_dimensions`, `recall_prior`) so the access is logged in the audit trail and the model's reads are as governed as its actions.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Even pure reads go through tools: `recall_prior` and `list_dimensions` are tool calls, not ambient resources, so every context fetch lands in the `audit_log` and the run stays fully reconstructable â€” a deliberate choice to keep auditability uniform across reads and writes.
**Follow-ups.** When would exposing the semantic registry as a resource beat exposing it via `get_metric`? / Does routing reads through tools cost you anything in latency or tokens? / How would prompts help if you added a self-serve drill-down chat surface?

#### Q9. How do you keep the byte budget and reconciliation guarantees from being bypassable through MCP?
**Ideal answer.** A guarantee is only as strong as its narrowest enforcement point, so I put both checks inside the one server that can execute â€” they cannot be routed around because there is no other executor. Every `run_query` is hard-capped at the configured `max_bytes_billed`, and crucially `run_query` refuses any SQL whose normalized hash was not seen by a prior `dry_run` in the same run, so the cost estimate that gated the call is the cost that gets billed. Reconciliation lives there too: aggregates are checked against canonical totals via `reconcile`, and >0.5% drift fails the finding. Because the agents hold no other path to bytes, the budget is an invariant of the system, not a hope.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** `warehouse-mcp.run_query` raises `NotDryRunFirst` for any un-dry-run'd SQL and `ByteBudgetExceeded` past the fixed 5 GiB cap; with the agents' allow-lists granting no alternative executor, the per-run budget and the dry-run-before-run-query gate are unbypassable.
**Follow-ups.** What stops an agent from issuing many small in-budget queries to blow the run total? / How does the same-run hash check survive query-cache or whitespace differences? / Where is the audit evidence that the budget was respected?

### Tool Calling

#### Q10. What makes a good tool schema for an LLM, and how did you design Helios's?
**Ideal answer.** A good tool schema is narrow, typed, and self-documenting: it accepts only canonical, validatable inputs, returns a structured typed result (never prose the model must re-parse), and names its error modes so failures are recoverable rather than silent. I prefer enums and registry-bound names over free strings, explicit windows over implicit "recent," and outputs shaped so the *next* tool can consume them directly. The schema should make the wrong call hard to express â€” if a parameter can only be a known dimension, the model cannot pass garbage. Concretely, `build_query` takes `(metric | [metric], dims, filters, window)` and returns `{sql, governed: true, metrics, dims}`, so its output drops straight into `dry_run`.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's schemas are registry-bound and chainable: `build_query`'s typed output feeds `dry_run`, whose `{total_bytes_processed}` gates `run_query`, whose row output is summarized into `decompose_change`'s `t0`/`t1` segment arrays â€” the schemas are designed as a pipeline, not isolated calls.
**Follow-ups.** Why pass a window object instead of letting the model write the date predicate? / How do you keep output schemas stable as you add metrics? / What error fields does a tool need for the model to self-correct?

#### Q11. Walk through the guardrail chain `build_query -> dry_run -> run_query`. Why this exact order?
**Ideal answer.** The order encodes "govern, then price, then spend." `build_query` first guarantees the SQL is *legal* â€” composed only from registry metrics and known dimensions, so it can reference only real columns. `dry_run` then guarantees it is *affordable* â€” BigQuery returns the exact bytes it would scan without executing, and the server records the SQL's normalized hash. `run_query` only then *spends*, and only on SQL that passed both gates: it rejects anything not previously dry-run'd in the same run and caps `max_bytes_billed`. Reversing any step breaks a guarantee â€” you cannot price ungoverned SQL safely, and you cannot trust a cost estimate you then let the model mutate before execution.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** This is grounding rule G3 made physical in `warehouse-mcp`: the same-run hash check ties the executed query to the one whose cost was approved, so the chain is `agent -> semantic.build_query -> warehouse.dry_run -> warehouse.run_query`, and only the warehouse server can complete it.
**Follow-ups.** What happens to the chain when `dry_run` reports over budget â€” retry, or something smarter? / Could the model dry-run a cheap query and run an expensive one? Why not? / Where does `reconcile` slot into this chain?

#### Q12. Why give the LLM *tools* instead of a database handle or a SQL string it can run?
**Ideal answer.** A database handle hands the model unbounded capability â€” arbitrary SQL, arbitrary cost, arbitrary tables, and an open door to hallucinated columns and runaway scans. Tools invert that: they expose a small set of safe, validated, budgeted operations, and the model composes *intent* (which metric, which dimensions, which window) rather than *implementation* (the SQL text). That preserves the three guarantees Helios sells â€” governed SQL, bounded cost, reconciled numbers â€” none of which can survive a raw handle. The model is good at deciding *what* to ask; it should never be trusted to author *how* against a billed, schema-fragile warehouse.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** No Helios agent holds a database handle or a raw-SQL execution tool; data is reached only by composing governed metrics through `semantic-mcp` and executing through `warehouse-mcp`'s gated `run_query`, which is exactly why a hallucinated column is structurally impossible.
**Follow-ups.** Doesn't forbidding raw SQL limit the questions the system can answer? How do you extend coverage? / What's your answer to "but text-to-SQL tools already do this"? / How would you handle a legitimately novel query the registry can't express yet?

#### Q13. Why do you insist that statistical tools be *deterministic*, and how do you guarantee it?
**Ideal answer.** Determinism means the same inputs always produce byte-identical outputs, which is non-negotiable for a system that must be reproducible, auditable, and regression-tested. If decomposition or significance were computed in token-space, the numbers would be non-reproducible, un-unit-testable, and silently wrong under paraphrase â€” you could never gate them in CI. So all math runs in real, version-pinned scipy/statsmodels/prophet code behind `stats-mcp`, with a fixed RNG seed for any stochastic method; the model passes arrays in and reads numbers out, never computing. This lets me unit-test the decomposition against hand-worked golden values and assert the same answer on every run.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** `stats-mcp.decompose_change` implements the exact identity `mix = Î£ Î”wÂ·r(t0)`, `rate = Î£ w(t0)Â·Î”r`, `interaction = Î£ Î”wÂ·Î”r`, seeded at `rng_seed: 1729`, so the mix/rate/interaction split is byte-reproducible and is one of the keystone transforms with golden-value tests.
**Follow-ups.** Which Helios statistics are actually stochastic, and how does the seed make them reproducible? / How would non-determinism here break your CI eval gate? / What's the failure mode if the model "rounds" a tool's number in the brief?

#### Q14. Sketch a tool-error taxonomy and the recovery strategy for each class.
**Ideal answer.** I group tool errors by the right *response*, not by where they originate. There are four useful classes: (1) **transient infrastructure** (warehouse/BigQuery flakiness, timeouts) â€” retry with bounded exponential backoff. (2) **budget/resource** (over-budget dry-run) â€” *do not* retry blindly; narrow the window or drop dimensions, then re-issue. (3) **governance/semantic** (unknown metric or dimension) â€” hard stop for that branch; re-plan against `list_dimensions`, never fall back to free SQL. (4) **data-quality/logic** (segment mismatch, zero sample, reconciliation drift) â€” fail the *finding*, not the run, and let the Critic record it. The discipline is that each class has one correct reaction, so the agent never papers over a governance error as if it were transient.

```text
TRANSIENT (QueryTimeout, 5xx)        -> retry x3, exponential backoff
BUDGET    (ByteBudgetExceeded)       -> narrow window/dims, re-dry_run (no blind retry)
GOVERNANCE(UnknownMetric/Dimension)  -> hard stop branch; re-plan via list_dimensions
DATA/LOGIC(SegmentMismatch, ZeroSample, reconcile >0.5%) -> fail finding, log, prune
```
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** This is encoded in the agent layer: a `dry_run` budget rejection (G3) forces scope-narrowing rather than a blind retry, an unknown-name error (G5) is a hard branch stop, and a single failed Diagnose branch is logged and pruned rather than aborting the run, while a >0.5% reconcile drift fails just that finding.
**Follow-ups.** Why is retrying an over-budget query specifically dangerous? / How do you stop a retry loop from blowing the 5-min/run SLA? / Which errors should surface to the brief versus stay in the audit log?

#### Q15. How do you prevent silent failures â€” a tool that returns plausible-but-wrong numbers?
**Ideal answer.** Silent failures are the dangerous ones because the model will confidently narrate them, so I defend with redundancy and reconciliation rather than trust. Every aggregate is reconciled against a canonical total (`reconcile`, >0.5% drift fails the finding), every finding is re-attacked by an independent adversarial agent, and every tool call â€” its args hash, the SQL run, bytes scanned, latency, verdict â€” is appended to an audit trail so a wrong number is traceable to the exact call that produced it. Keystone transforms that fail silently (sessionization, the decomposition, revenue) get golden-value tests so a regression trips CI rather than shipping. The principle is verify-then-trust: a number earns its place in the brief by surviving an independent check.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Revenue must reconcile to the cent (the `revenue_reconciles` test), `decompose_change` is golden-tested, and the Critic re-runs `significance_test` on holdout slices and probes for confounds before a finding can ship â€” three independent silent-failure tripwires.
**Follow-ups.** Why is the decomposition specifically prone to silent failure? / How does an independent Critic catch what unit tests can't? / What's your "fail loud" path when reconciliation drifts?

#### Q16. How do you keep large tool outputs (row sets) from poisoning the context window?
**Ideal answer.** The model should see *aggregates and structure*, never raw row dumps â€” both to control cost and to prevent the model from re-deriving statistics it should be calling tools for. So tool outputs that could be large are summarized to the shape the next step needs before they enter the LLM's context: a query that returns thousands of rows is collapsed to per-segment `(weight, rate)` arrays for the decomposition, not streamed verbatim. Inter-stage hand-off is a compact typed JSON envelope, not prose, so token usage stays roughly flat even as the dimensional search widens. This keeps each agent's context small, cheap, and focused on decisions rather than data.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Raw query result sets are summarized to aggregates before entering context â€” the LLM never sees row-level dumps â€” and stages hand off the typed Finding envelope (`finding_id`, `metric`, `dims`, `t0`, `t1`, `decomposition`, `significance`, `dollar_impact`, `evidence_query_ids`), so token usage stays flat as the hypothesis tree expands.
**Follow-ups.** Where does the summarization happen â€” in the tool, the agent, or the control plane? / How do you keep `evidence_query_ids` enough to reconstruct without re-embedding the rows? / What breaks if a row set is summarized too aggressively?

#### Q17. How do you test tools in isolation from the unreliable LLM and the live warehouse?
**Ideal answer.** Tools are ordinary typed functions, so they get ordinary deterministic tests â€” independent of the model and, where possible, of the warehouse. Pure-compute servers (stats, experiment) are unit-tested against hand-worked golden values with a fixed seed, with zero warehouse dependency. The semantic server is tested by asserting that `build_query` emits the expected governed SQL for known metric/dimension combinations and rejects unknown names, without executing anything. Warehouse interactions are validated via `dry_run` (no spend) and golden-row fixtures, and the full pipeline is graded end-to-end by the offline eval harness on a frozen dataset â€” so a tool regression is caught at the unit level *and* the system level before it can reach a real run.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** `stats-mcp` runs entirely on stdio with no credentials precisely so it is unit-testable in isolation, and the keystones â€” `decompose_change`, sessionization, revenue reconciliation â€” carry golden-value tests, while the CI eval gate grades the assembled pipeline on 50 frozen labeled scenarios.
**Follow-ups.** How do you write a golden test for a stochastic forecast? / How do you test the *boundary* â€” that `run_query` truly refuses un-dry-run'd SQL? / What's the cheapest test that would have caught a hallucinated-column regression?

### Agent Orchestration

#### Q18. Why a deterministic finite-state machine with model-driven *nodes*, rather than letting the LLM decide the flow?
**Ideal answer.** Control flow and reasoning are different jobs and should be owned by different things. Letting an LLM decide *what to do next* (an open agent loop) is non-reproducible, hard to budget, and hard to test â€” the same input can take different paths on different runs. So I make the *flow* deterministic code â€” a finite-state machine with named states and explicit transitions â€” while the LLM is a *composer within a node*: at each state it decides which tool calls to emit, but it cannot change the order of stages or skip the Critic. This gives the reasoning flexibility of agents (a best-first hypothesis search, an adversarial refutation) with the reproducibility, auditability, and cost control of a fixed pipeline. The slogan: the model composes tool calls; it never controls flow.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The Orchestrator drives a fixed FSM â€” `PLAN -> MONITOR -> (CLEAN_RUN | DECOMPOSE -> DIAGNOSE -> CRITIC LOOP -> PRESCRIBE -> NARRATE) -> END` â€” and worker output never ships directly; it must pass the Critic loop, so the diagnosis path is identical and reconstructable every run.
**Follow-ups.** What concretely becomes untestable if the LLM controls transitions? / How do you handle a genuinely novel case the FSM has no state for? / Where in a node does the model still have real latitude?

#### Q19. Why seven agents instead of one well-prompted model?
**Ideal answer.** Specialization and adversarial review are the two things a single prompt cannot do well. A monolithic prompt asked to both confidently propose a root cause *and* rigorously refute it is in conflict with itself â€” confirmation bias is structural. Splitting into role-specialized agents lets each have a tight system prompt, a minimal tool allow-list, and an isolated, testable responsibility, and lets one agent be purpose-built to *attack* another's output. It also lets me right-size the model per role and keep each agent's context small. The cost is orchestration complexity and inter-agent latency, which the deterministic FSM and typed hand-offs keep bounded. Multi-agent here is not fashion; it is how I get an adversarial check and least-privilege at the same time.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The seven agents (Orchestrator, Monitor, Decompose, Diagnose, Prescribe, Narrator, Critic) each own one stage with its own allow-list, and the Critic exists specifically to refute the Diagnose agent â€” a separation a single prompt cannot honestly reproduce.
**Follow-ups.** Where is the latency cost of seven hops, and is it inside the 5-min budget? / Which two agents could you merge with least harm, and which must never merge? / How do you stop the agents from duplicating each other's queries?

#### Q20. How did you decide Opus versus Sonnet per agent?
**Ideal answer.** I matched model capability to the reasoning depth and cost-of-error of each role, not to seniority for its own sake. Opus goes to the three roles that search an open hypothesis space or carry a high cost of being wrong: the Orchestrator (global planning, budget allocation, branch routing), Diagnose (combinatorial hypothesis-tree search with self-directed SQL verification), and the Critic (adversarial reasoning that must out-think Diagnose to find a confound). Sonnet handles the high-volume, bounded-action-space stages â€” Monitor, Decompose, Prescribe, Narrator â€” where the decision is "which series to test," "which decomposition to run," "which power analysis to call," "which template to fill." This keeps cost per run inside the fixed budget while spending the expensive model only where judgment actually moves accuracy.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The Opus/Sonnet split is exactly Orchestrator/Diagnose/Critic on Opus and Monitor/Decompose/Prescribe/Narrator on Sonnet â€” reserving the costly model for open-ended reasoning and adversarial critique, the two places error is most expensive.
**Follow-ups.** Could the Decompose agent be Sonnet *because* the math is in a deterministic tool â€” is that the reason? / When would you promote Monitor to Opus? / How do you measure whether an Opus role is worth its cost?

#### Q21. Explain the Critic loop â€” verdicts, the retry bound, and why an adversarial agent matters.
**Ideal answer.** The Critic is a dedicated adversary that tries to *refute* every candidate finding along the four classic failure modes â€” mix confound, insufficient sample, seasonality, and data-quality artifact â€” and returns one of three verdicts. PASS: the finding survived refutation and proceeds to Prescribe. DOWNGRADE: plausible but under-evidenced (sample too small, decomposition not isolated to one dimension), so it returns to Diagnose for a *targeted* re-query, bounded by `MAX_REFUTE_ROUNDS = 2`; after the cap it is demoted to a watchlist note rather than a confident finding. DROP: refuted (confound found, fails reconciliation, fully explained by known seasonality/launch from memory), discarded and locally suppressed so siblings don't re-raise it. The bound matters because an unbounded refute loop would blow the time and byte budgets; the adversary matters because the cheapest way to make a finding trustworthy is to try hard to break it first.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The Critic (Opus) consults `recall_prior` for seasonality/launch and suppression, re-runs `significance_test` on holdout slices, and probes for a traffic-mix confound via `run_query` â€” a structurally separate refutation pass that is the third defensibility pillar alongside governed SQL and deterministic stats.
**Follow-ups.** Why cap at two refute rounds specifically â€” what happens at the cap? / How does the Critic avoid simply agreeing with Diagnose? / What does a DROP add to suppression, and how is that scoped to the run?

#### Q22. What is the Finding envelope, why typed JSON, and how does it enable hand-off?
**Ideal answer.** The Finding envelope is the single typed contract that every stage produces and consumes, so agents communicate via structured data, not prose. It carries the identity and full evidentiary state of a candidate: the metric, dimension slice, time window, the mix/rate/interaction decomposition, the significance result, the dollar revenue-at-risk, and pointers to the evidence queries. Typed JSON matters for three reasons: it is validatable (a malformed finding fails fast), it is compact (token usage stays flat as the search widens), and it is the same shape the memory store persists â€” so a finding flows from Diagnose through Critic to Narrator to `save_diagnosis` without re-parsing. Prose hand-off would be lossy, unvalidatable, and would tempt downstream agents to re-derive numbers they should inherit.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The hand-off envelope is exactly `{finding_id, metric, dims, t0, t1, decomposition, significance, dollar_impact, evidence_query_ids}`, and it maps onto the `diagnosis_history` schema so the same typed object the Critic vets is what the Narrator renders and `save_diagnosis` persists.
**Follow-ups.** Why include `evidence_query_ids` rather than the rows themselves? / What validation runs on the envelope between stages? / How does the envelope's shape line up with what memory needs to recall later?

#### Q23. How does context compaction work across the pipeline, and why is it load-bearing?
**Ideal answer.** Compaction means each agent receives the *minimum* context it needs to act, summarized and structured, rather than the accumulated transcript of the whole run. Concretely a worker gets: the run plan, the canonical metric/dimension catalog, the upstream stage's typed output (JSON, not prose), and any priors from `recall_prior` â€” and raw result sets are reduced to aggregates before they enter context. This is load-bearing for two reasons: cost and time (a flat, small context per agent keeps a multi-agent run inside the <5-min and token budgets), and *quality* (a focused context reduces distraction and keeps the model on its one job). Without compaction, a deep hypothesis-tree search would balloon context linearly with the number of branches and both cost and latency would blow.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios compacts deliberately â€” summarized aggregates instead of row dumps, typed Finding envelopes instead of prose, and `recall_prior` priors decayed by `exp(-age_days/HALF_LIFE)` â€” so token usage stays flat even as Diagnose widens its dimensional search to the bounded `MAX_BRANCHING = 4`, `MAX_DEPTH = 3`.
**Follow-ups.** What information is safe to drop in compaction, and what must always survive? / How does memory decay interact with context size over many runs? / How would you detect that compaction silently dropped a needed fact?

#### Q24. How does the FSM handle failure isolation, retries, and the clean-run case?
**Ideal answer.** A robust pipeline degrades gracefully and never ships a partial result. Failure is isolated by scope: a single failed Diagnose branch is logged and pruned, not fatal, because one dead hypothesis shouldn't kill the run; but if Monitor or Decompose fail entirely, the Orchestrator aborts and writes an audit record rather than emitting a half-built brief. Retries are class-specific: transient warehouse errors back off and retry three times, a budget rejection forces scope-narrowing instead of a blind retry, and a governance error hard-stops that branch. The clean-run case is first-class: if Monitor finds no anomaly, the FSM short-circuits to a "no material change" brief and *still* writes a run-state audit row, so absence-of-finding is itself recorded and the run is reproducible.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The FSM's `CLEAN_RUN -> NARRATE(no-finding brief) -> END` branch and the abort-with-audit-on-Monitor/Decompose-failure path are explicit, and every tool call lands in `audit_log` (args hash, SQL, bytes, verdict) so even a clean or aborted run is fully reconstructable.
**Follow-ups.** Why record a clean run at all? / How do you prevent a flaky branch from silently degrading accuracy? / What's the recovery if the Orchestrator itself crashes mid-run?

#### Q25. How do you keep an autonomous multi-agent run cheap, bounded, and on-time?
**Ideal answer.** Autonomy is only safe if it is bounded, so cost and time are enforced at every layer rather than hoped for. The byte budget is hard-capped per `run_query` and the dry-run gate prices every query before it spends; the hypothesis search is breadth- and depth-bounded so it cannot fan out indefinitely; the Critic loop is capped at two refute rounds; the model split spends Opus only on reasoning-critical roles; and context compaction keeps token cost flat. The Orchestrator also watches the running byte total and tightens scope mid-run if it nears the cap. Together these turn "an AI agent loose on a warehouse" into a run with a known worst-case cost and a sub-five-minute SLA â€” which is what makes scheduling it unattended defensible.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The fixed 5 GiB byte budget (`byte_budget: 5368709120`), `MAX_BRANCHING = 4` / `MAX_DEPTH = 3` in Diagnose, `MAX_REFUTE_ROUNDS = 2` in the Critic, the Opus/Sonnet split, and mid-run scope-tightening off the `run_state.bytes_scanned` counter jointly hold each run under 5 minutes and inside budget.
**Follow-ups.** Which bound binds first in practice â€” bytes, time, or tokens? / How do you choose the depth/branching caps without hurting the 85% accuracy target? / What's your alerting if a scheduled run breaches its budget?

---

### Evaluation

#### Q26. How do you evaluate an autonomous diagnosis engine when there's no labeled "right answer" lying around in production?
**Ideal answer.** You manufacture ground truth. The general technique is to take a frozen real dataset, inject anomalies whose root cause you authored, and score whether the system rediscovers exactly what you planted â€” turning an unmeasurable open-ended task into a closed-form accuracy problem. I built an offline labeled benchmark: a frozen copy of the GA4 dataset into which I perturb specific segments, with a YAML label recording the true cause (which segment, mix vs rate, dollar impact), then run the full pipeline and grade its top-ranked finding against that label. This is strictly better than eyeballing a few outputs because it's repeatable, regression-gateable in CI, and reports a number you can defend. The alternative â€” LLM-as-judge over unlabeled prod runs â€” measures plausibility, not correctness, and an engine that confidently invents plausible-but-wrong causes is the exact failure I'm trying to kill.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The `helios.eval` harness (`injector.py` -> `runner.py` -> `scorers/`) grades against authored labels in `scenarios/*.yaml` over a frozen `helios_eval` dataset, and the gate lives in CI.
**Follow-ups.** Why is a frozen dataset copy essential â€” what breaks if you inject into live data? / How do you keep labels honest so you aren't grading the system against your own confirmation bias? / What's the cost of maintaining a labeled benchmark as the schema evolves?

#### Q27. Walk me through synthetic anomaly injection. How do you inject a *mix-shift* differently from a *rate-change*?
**Ideal answer.** The two perturbation modes map directly onto the decomposition identity `Î”R = Î£ Î”w_iÂ·r_i(t0) + Î£ w_i(t0)Â·Î”r_i + Î£ Î”w_iÂ·Î”r_i`, so I inject each by moving exactly one term. A **rate perturbation** holds segment volume shares `w_i` constant and depresses one segment's conversion `r_i` (e.g. drop mobile/Chrome `checkout_to_purchase_rate` by 20% by deleting a fraction of that segment's downstream `reached_*` flags) â€” true behavior change. A **volume/mix perturbation** holds every segment's `r_i` constant and reweights traffic `w_i` (e.g. shift session share from high-converting Direct to low-converting Display by duplicating/dropping whole sessions) â€” composition artifact with no behavior change. The discipline is that injection touches *one* lever so the label is unambiguous: a correct system must attribute a rate injection to `rate_effect` and a mix injection to `mix_effect`, and the naive "biggest absolute segment delta" baseline conflates them. The label stores the segment, the mode, the magnitude, and the derived dollar-at-risk so I can score cause, classification, *and* MAPE.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** `eval/injector.py` implements exactly two primitives â€” rate-perturbation (mutate `r_i` via `reached_*` flags) and volume-perturbation (mutate `w_i` via session resampling) â€” so each scenario's label is a clean mix-or-rate ground truth.
**Follow-ups.** How do you prevent an injection from also accidentally moving a parent metric and contaminating a "single-cause" label? / Why inject by editing `reached_*` flags rather than the final rate column? / How do you inject a seasonality *decoy* that should NOT be flagged?

#### Q28. Describe the 50-scenario suite. Why those buckets, and what failure does each one police?
**Ideal answer.** Coverage matters more than count â€” a benchmark is only as good as the failure modes it can catch, so I designed seven buckets, not 50 random anomalies. They are: single-segment rate change (the base case), multi-segment rate change (additivity under several simultaneous drops), single-segment mix shift and multi-segment mix shift (Simpson's-paradox traps), seasonality decoys (a real-looking dip that is calendar-explained and must NOT be alarmed), no-anomaly controls (the system must stay silent â€” guards false-positive rate), and data-quality artifacts (a tracking gap or duplicate that looks like a drop but is an ingestion bug). The mix buckets specifically police the engine's core thesis: a baseline that chases the largest delta will misattribute composition artifacts to behavior. The controls and decoys police the opposite failure â€” an always-on agent that cries wolf gets muted, so silence-when-correct is a graded outcome, not an afterthought.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The `scenarios/` set spans all 7 buckets; the no-anomaly and seasonality-decoy buckets are what let me report a real precision number and prove the Critic earns its keep.
**Follow-ups.** Why include data-quality artifacts at all â€” isn't that the warehouse's job? / How do you weight buckets when computing a headline score? / What's the minimum scenarios-per-bucket for the accuracy number to be statistically meaningful?

#### Q29. What exactly do you measure per run? Give me the metric definitions, not just names.
**Ideal answer.** Five graded quantities, each tied to a trust pillar. (1) **Root-cause segment accuracy** = fraction of scenarios where the top-1 promoted finding names the injected segment *and* the correct mix/rate classification (the headline). (2) **Decomposition MAPE** = mean absolute percentage error of `stats-mcp`'s reported mix/rate/interaction magnitudes against the injected dollar/rate deltas â€” graded `â‰¤10%`. (3) **Detection F1** over the anomaly-vs-control split, so missed anomalies and false alarms both cost. (4) **Hallucination rate** = count of columns/metrics in emitted SQL absent from the governed registry â€” a hard-zero gate. (5) **Faithfulness** = fraction of numeric claims in the Decision Brief that are verbatim tool outputs traceable to an `audit_log` row, not paraphrases. Accuracy is the product number; MAPE validates the math pillar; F1 validates detection; hallucination and faithfulness validate the grounding pillar.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Each metric is a scorer under `eval/scorers/` (`accuracy.py`, `decomposition.py`, `detection.py`, `hallucination.py`, `faithfulness.py`); the headline targets are `â‰¥85%` accuracy, `â‰¤10%` MAPE, `0` hallucinations.
**Follow-ups.** Why top-1 and not top-3 recall for the headline? / How do you score a finding that's right on segment but wrong on dollar magnitude? / How is faithfulness scored mechanically without another LLM in the loop?

#### Q30. Define the naive baseline precisely. Why 85% vs 45%, and why is that gap the whole point?
**Ideal answer.** The baseline must be the thing a competent analyst or a junior tool would actually do, or the comparison is a strawman. Mine is **"largest absolute segment delta"**: cross-tab the metric by each dimension, rank segments by `|Î”R_segment|`, and report the biggest mover as the cause â€” no decomposition, no significance, no mix/rate split. It scores around 45% because it gets single-segment rate changes roughly right but is *systematically* fooled by mix shifts (it flags the segment whose volume moved, not the one whose behavior moved) and by small-but-significant changes it under-ranks. Helios hits `â‰¥85%` because `decompose_change` separates the levers and Diagnose drills rate-effects first. The gap is the entire product thesis quantified: the hard part isn't surfacing a number, it's attributing it correctly, and 40 points of accuracy is the dollar value of doing the decomposition properly.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The baseline runs in the same harness over the same scenarios, so `85 vs 45` is an apples-to-apples number, and the mix buckets are precisely where the gap opens.
**Follow-ups.** Could you make a stronger baseline (e.g. delta normalized by segment volume) and does the gap survive? / On which bucket does the baseline come closest to Helios? / Why is "largest delta" the right strawman for *this* product rather than a random baseline?

#### Q31. How does eval run in CI, and what exactly does it gate?
**Ideal answer.** A benchmark that isn't a merge gate is a vanity metric, so I wired it into GitHub Actions with two tiers. Every push runs a **smoke subset** (a fast representative slice across buckets) for quick signal; every PR to `main` runs the **full suite**. The gate (`eval/gates.yaml`) blocks the merge if top-1 accuracy regresses more than ~2 points from the baseline-on-main, if hallucination is anything but zero, if decomposition MAPE exceeds 10%, or if a run breaks the 5 GiB byte budget or the <5-min latency SLA. Crucially the *same* `hallucination.py` scorer that gates CI also runs over the production `audit_log`, so the offline invariant and the online observation are enforced by one piece of code. This makes correctness a property the repo protects mechanically, not a claim that quietly rots.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** `.github/workflows/ci.yml` invokes `eval.runner --smoke` on push and `--full` on PR-to-main; `eval/gates.yaml` encodes the accuracy/hallucination/MAPE/budget thresholds as hard gates.
**Follow-ups.** Why a 2-point regression tolerance and not zero? / How do you keep full-suite eval cost inside a CI budget given BigQuery scans? / How do you stop the benchmark from being silently overfit by repeated tuning against it?

#### Q32. What stops you from overfitting the agents to your own 50 scenarios?
**Ideal answer.** A benchmark you tune against eventually measures memorization, so I treat it like a held-out set with hygiene rules. I keep the injection *primitives* generic (perturb any `(segment, mode, magnitude)`) rather than hand-crafting bespoke cases the agents could pattern-match, and I parameterize scenarios so I can resample fresh segment/magnitude combinations the prompts have never seen. I separate a smoke subset used during development from the full suite that gates merges, and I watch per-bucket scores, not just the headline, so improving rate-cases by breaking mix-cases shows up immediately. The honest limitation I'd state in an interview: 50 scenarios over one observational dataset is a starting harness, not a guarantee of generalization, and the right next step is randomized scenario generation plus a second dataset.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The `injector` is primitive-based and parameterized rather than a fixture of frozen answers, and the smoke/full split (`--smoke` vs `--full`) keeps a portion out of the daily tuning loop.
**Follow-ups.** How would you build a true train/test split for scenarios? / What signal tells you you've started overfitting? / Would you trust the 85% on a second e-commerce dataset, and why or why not?

#### Q33. Why is an offline benchmark a stronger artifact than a slick live demo?
**Ideal answer.** A demo proves the system *can* be right once; a benchmark proves how often it *is* right, and the second is what earns trust for an unattended agent. Live demos are cherry-picked, non-reproducible, and silent about failure modes â€” they answer "does it look impressive" not "what's the error rate on mix-shift traps." The benchmark gives me a defensible distribution: 85% top-1, zero hallucinations, MAPE under 10%, against a stated 45% baseline, reproducible on every commit. For autonomous tooling specifically this matters more than for a chatbot, because no human is in the loop to catch a wrong answer at runtime â€” the only place correctness can be established is offline, in advance. In an interview the benchmark also lets me discuss failure honestly, which reads as senior judgment rather than salesmanship.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The headline is reported as a graded distribution from `eval.runner`, not a screenshot â€” the trust story is "measured 85% vs 45%," which is the third defensibility pillar (adversarial verification + offline eval).
**Follow-ups.** When IS a live demo the better artifact? / How do you communicate eval results to a non-technical stakeholder? / What would change your headline metric if a customer's funnel looked nothing like the Merch Store?

### Prompt Engineering

#### Q34. How are the system prompts structured across the seven agents â€” one mega-prompt or per-agent?
**Ideal answer.** Per-agent, narrow, and role-scoped â€” a single mega-prompt invites the model to do everything badly and makes failures unattributable. Each agent gets a system prompt that states only its job, its allowed tools, its output contract, and the rules it must obey, so the prompt surface for any one node is small and testable. The Monitor's prompt knows only "call `detect_anomaly`, emit a typed Finding or 'clean'"; the Diagnose prompt knows the best-first search policy and the rate-before-mix ordering; the Critic's prompt is purely adversarial â€” "try to refute this finding via these four attack templates." The shared spine across all prompts is the grounding contract (G1-G5) and the typed "Finding" JSON envelope every agent reads and writes. Smaller role-scoped prompts also let me pick model tier per agent (Opus for Diagnose/Critic/Orchestrator reasoning, Sonnet for the mechanical nodes), which is both cheaper and more reliable.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Seven distinct agents (`helios/agents/`) each carry a focused system prompt plus a per-agent MCP tool allow-list (Bible Â§18.9) â€” e.g. the Narrator's prompt and allow-list both forbid `run_query`.
**Follow-ups.** How do you keep seven prompts consistent without copy-paste drift? / Why give Diagnose and Critic Opus but Narrator Sonnet? / How do you version and test a prompt change without a full eval run?

#### Q35. State the grounding rules G1-G5 and explain which are behavioral and which are structural.
**Ideal answer.** **G1** never emit raw SQL â€” get data only via `semantic-mcp.build_query`/`get_metric`. **G2** never compute a statistic in prose â€” anomaly, decomposition, significance, forecast, power come only from `stats-mcp`/`experiment-mcp`, and numbers in the brief are tool outputs verbatim. **G3** `warehouse-mcp.dry_run` before every `run_query`; over-budget means narrow the query, not retry blindly. **G4** reconcile aggregates via `warehouse-mcp.reconcile`; >0.5% drift fails the finding. **G5** use only canonical metric/dimension names â€” an unknown name is a hard error, never a fallback to free SQL. The distinction that matters: stating these in a prompt is *behavioral* (the model is asked to comply) but Helios also enforces them *structurally* â€” the agent literally has no SQL tool, only `semantic-mcp`; the per-agent allow-list makes `run_query` uncallable from a writer agent; the byte gate and reconcile run in `warehouse-mcp` regardless of what the model "intends." The prompt makes compliance the path of least resistance; the architecture makes violation impossible.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** G1-G5 are documented as the five non-negotiables in `CLAUDE.md`, and each has a structural counterpart: no raw-SQL tool exists, allow-lists gate tools, `dry_run`/`reconcile` are mandatory in `warehouse-mcp`.
**Follow-ups.** Which of G1-G5 would still hold if the prompt were blank, and which wouldn't? / What's the failure mode if G3's dry_run is advisory rather than enforced? / How does G4's 0.5% threshold interact with floating-point rounding in the warehouse?

#### Q36. Why do you claim "structural beats prompt-only"? Convince me a prompt isn't enough.
**Ideal answer.** A prompt is a *request* to a probabilistic system; under distribution shift, long context, or an adversarial input it will eventually be violated, and for an unattended agent "eventually" means "in production, unobserved." The senior move is to make the desired property a structural invariant so violation isn't a low-probability event, it's impossible. Concretely: I can ask the model in a prompt to "only use governed metrics" (prompt-only) â€” or I can give the agent no SQL-execution tool at all and route every data request through `semantic-mcp`, so the model *cannot* express a hallucinated column even if it wanted to (structural). Prompt-only gets you maybe 99% compliance; structural gets you 100% and, more importantly, gets you *provability* â€” I can scan `audit_log.sql_text` and assert every column came from the registry. The tradeoff is real: structural governance is more upfront engineering than a paragraph of instructions, and it constrains what the model can do, which is exactly why it's safe.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Helios's hallucination rate is a hard-zero CI gate, achievable only because `semantic-mcp` is the *sole* SQL path and the registry is the single source of truth â€” the model never holds a SQL pen.
**Follow-ups.** Give an example where prompt-only grounding silently fails at 99% compliance. / Does structural governance ever block a legitimate analysis the model should do? / How do you add a new metric without loosening the structural gate?

#### Q37. Walk me through writing the Critic's prompt. How do you prompt a model to attack rather than agree?
**Ideal answer.** LLMs are sycophantic by default â€” they want to ratify the finding handed to them â€” so the Critic's prompt inverts the objective: its job is to *refute*, and a finding survives only by surviving attack. I give it a fixed set of attack templates rather than open-ended "is this right?", because named adversarial moves are testable and reproducible: mix-confound (is this composition not behavior?), insufficient-sample (does `significance_test` actually clear?), seasonality (is this calendar-explained?), and data-quality (is this a tracking artifact?). The prompt forces a structured verdict â€” PASS, DOWNGRADE, or DROP â€” with a cited reason, and routes every check through tools (re-run `decompose_change`, consult the `seasonality_calendar`) so the refutation is grounded, not rhetorical. Framing it as "earn the finding's survival" rather than "grade the finding" is what flips the model out of agreement mode.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The Critic (Opus) runs four named refutation templates and emits a PASS/DOWNGRADE/DROP verdict per the Finding envelope; it consults memory's `seasonality_calendar`/`launch_calendar` and re-runs `stats-mcp` rather than judging in prose.
**Follow-ups.** How do you stop the Critic from being so aggressive it drops every real finding? / How do you eval the Critic in isolation? / Why named attack templates instead of a free-form adversarial prompt?

#### Q38. What does refusal of free SQL look like in practice â€” show me the path when the model wants a column that doesn't exist.
**Ideal answer.** The model never gets to "want a column" in SQL terms because the only data verb it can call is `semantic-mcp.build_query(metric, dims, filters, window)` over canonical names. If it composes a call referencing a name not in the registry, `semantic-mcp` returns a hard error listing the valid names â€” there is no escape hatch that drops to hand-written SQL (G5). For example:
```
build_query(metric="add_to_cart_conversion", dims=["device"])
-> ERROR: unknown metric "add_to_cart_conversion" (did you mean "view_to_cart_rate"?);
   unknown dimension "device" (did you mean "device_category"?). No SQL emitted.
```
The agent's correct response is to re-compose with valid names, not to route around the gate, because no around exists â€” there's no `run_sql` tool in its allow-list. This is the structural enforcement of G1/G5: a hallucinated column is caught at compose time as a typed error, never reaching the warehouse, which is why the eval can claim a hard zero on hallucinations.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** `semantic-mcp.build_query` validates every metric/dimension against `semantic_layer.yaml` (47 metrics + 19 dimensions, referential-integrity compiled) and errors on unknowns; no agent has a raw-SQL tool.
**Follow-ups.** Should the error suggest near-matches, and does that risk the model rationalizing a wrong metric? / What if the analysis genuinely needs a metric not yet in the registry? / How does this differ from a text-to-SQL system's "validate after generate" approach?

#### Q39. How do you prompt for the typed "Finding" envelope, and why a typed handoff at all?
**Ideal answer.** Free-text handoffs between agents lose structure and let errors compound silently, so every agent reads and writes one typed JSON "Finding" â€” segment, metric, mix/rate classification, magnitudes, significance, dollar impact, Critic verdict, and provenance. The prompt specifies the schema and instructs the agent to emit *only* valid JSON conforming to it, and I validate the output against the schema before the FSM advances â€” a malformed envelope is a node failure, not a downstream surprise. This makes the pipeline composable (Diagnose's output is exactly Critic's input), debuggable (every field is inspectable in `audit_log`), and lets the deterministic FSM, not prose, control flow. The envelope is also what decouples the agents from the data: they pass typed findings, never rows, which is why `stats-mcp` and the workers are stateless and replicable.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The typed "Finding" envelope is the contract handed between the seven agents on the FSM; validating it at each node is what lets the Orchestrator drive flow deterministically rather than parsing model prose.
**Follow-ups.** What do you do when the model emits prose around the JSON? / How do you evolve the Finding schema without breaking older diagnoses in memory? / Why JSON over a tool-call/function-calling structured output?

### Memory Systems

#### Q40. Describe Helios's memory. What's persisted, and in what stores?
**Ideal answer.** Memory turns a one-shot analyst into one that learns across runs, so I persist four things in `helios_memory` (BigQuery) plus a vector store. (1) **Diagnosis history** â€” every shipped finding with its embedding, so a new finding can be matched against priors semantically, not just by exact segment match. (2) A **suppression list** â€” acknowledged or auto-suppressed findings with a reason and TTL, so the engine stops re-alarming on known issues. (3) **Seasonality and launch calendars** â€” known periodic effects and product/marketing events the Critic uses to refute calendar-explained dips. (4) An **action-tracking** table â€” the prescribed experiment, its status, and the measured readback, closing the did-the-fix-work loop. BigQuery holds the structured rows and durable state of record; the vector store holds embeddings for semantic recall. Crucially, all cross-run learning lives in these stores, never in an agent's heap, which is what keeps the agents stateless and the system restartable.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** `helios_memory` + vector store hold diagnosis history (embeddings), suppression list, seasonality/launch calendars, and action-tracking; `report-mcp` exposes `save_diagnosis`/`recall_prior` as the memory interface.
**Follow-ups.** Why split structured rows (BigQuery) from embeddings (vector store)? / What's the schema of the diagnosis-history row? / How does keeping state out of the agent heap help horizontal scale?

#### Q41. How does `recall_prior` work, and how does it change a diagnosis?
**Ideal answer.** `recall_prior` is semantic retrieval over diagnosis history: given the current finding (segment, metric, movement), it embeds the query and returns the nearest prior diagnoses plus their outcomes and any suppression/calendar matches. This changes the diagnosis three ways â€” it lets Diagnose say "we've seen this mobile-Safari checkout drop before, here's what we found and whether the fix worked," it lets the Critic check whether this exact issue is suppressed or calendar-explained before promoting it, and it lets the Narrator add continuity ("recurring, third time this quarter") instead of treating every run as amnesiac. The retrieval is semantic so it matches *related* priors (a Safari issue surfacing the prior Chrome-mobile finding), not just byte-identical ones. The guardrail: recall *informs* but never *overrides* the live decomposition â€” priors are evidence the current tools must still confirm, so a stale prior can't manufacture a finding the data doesn't support.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** `report-mcp.recall_prior` performs embedding-based retrieval over the diagnosis history; Diagnose and the Critic both consult it before promoting a leaf.
**Follow-ups.** How do you stop a confident prior from biasing the model toward a wrong-but-familiar cause? / What's your embedding model and similarity threshold? / How do you handle a prior whose schema/metrics have since changed?

#### Q42. Explain the suppression list and its TTL. Why is "don't re-alarm" a memory problem?
**Ideal answer.** An always-on engine that re-reports the same known issue every run gets muted by its operators â€” so suppression is the difference between a tool people keep running and one they turn off. When a stakeholder acknowledges a finding (or the Critic auto-suppresses a known artifact), I write a suppression row with the segment/metric signature, a human reason, and a TTL (default 30 days). Diagnose and Critic consult it before promoting a leaf; a match is auto-DROPped with reason "suppressed." The TTL is the subtle part: suppression must *expire* so a genuinely re-emerging problem eventually re-surfaces rather than being silenced forever â€” a permanent mute is how you miss the second, real occurrence. This is a memory problem because it's cross-run state about operator intent, which can't live in a stateless agent or be re-derived from the data alone.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** The TTL'd suppression list in `helios_memory` is consulted by Diagnose and the Critic; a match yields an auto-DROP verdict with reason "suppressed."
**Follow-ups.** Why 30 days and not indefinite or 7 days? / How do you distinguish "suppress this instance" from "suppress this pattern forever"? / What happens if a suppressed issue's dollar impact suddenly triples within the TTL?

#### Q43. How does the action-tracking loop close, and why does it make Helios more than a reporter?
**Ideal answer.** A diagnosis without a feedback loop is a reporter; closing the loop is what makes it an analyst that's accountable for its prescriptions. When Prescribe ships an experiment card, I write an action-tracking row (the recommended action, the targeted segment/metric, the expected effect, status). Because the dataset is observational, Helios can't run a live A/B, so it closes the loop with a *quasi-experimental readback* â€” pre/post or difference-in-differences on the segment after the action's stated date â€” and writes the measured outcome back to the row. That outcome then feeds future `recall_prior` calls, so the next time a similar issue appears the engine knows whether its prior fix actually worked. This is the did-the-fix-work loop, and it's what lets Helios improve its prescriptions over time instead of repeating advice that didn't land.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Action-tracking in `helios_memory` plus the honest constraint that the data is observational (Nov 2020-Jan 2021) â€” so Helios designs/sizes experiments and runs pre/post or diff-in-diff readbacks rather than live A/Bs.
**Follow-ups.** Why is diff-in-diff a defensible readback on observational data, and where does it break? / How do you attribute a metric move to your action vs confounders? / What do you do when the readback says the fix didn't work?

#### Q44. Memory grows unbounded. How do you handle decay, staleness, and relevance over time?
**Ideal answer.** Unbounded memory degrades both cost and quality â€” stale priors mislead recall and the byte budget balloons â€” so memory needs decay just like a cache. Suppression entries decay via their TTL. Diagnosis history I'd weight by recency and by whether the action-tracking readback confirmed the diagnosis, so a confirmed recent finding outranks an old unconfirmed one in `recall_prior`; outright pruning or archiving very old, low-relevance diagnoses keeps the vector index lean. Seasonality entries are inherently periodic so they persist, but launch-calendar entries age out once their effect window passes. The principle is that recall relevance should reflect *current* truth, and that means actively forgetting: a year-old finding about a since-redesigned checkout is noise, not signal. Honestly, at 3 months of data this is more design-for-scale than a present pain, but it's the right thing to build before memory is large.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** TTL'd suppression plus recency/confirmation-weighted recall over the embedded diagnosis history â€” decay is a first-class property of the memory schema, not an afterthought.
**Follow-ups.** How would you measure whether decay is helping or hurting recall quality? / Should a *contradicted* prior be deleted or kept as a negative example? / How do you bound the vector store's growth in a multi-tenant deployment?

### Architecture Decisions

#### Q45. The biggest decision: grounding over generation. Defend it against "just let a strong model write the SQL."
**Ideal answer.** Letting a strong model free-text SQL optimizes for *capability demo*; grounding optimizes for *correctness at scale*, and for an unattended engine the second is the only one that matters. A model writing SQL will, at some rate, hallucinate a column, mis-define a rate as an average-of-ratios (re-introducing Simpson's paradox), or silently change a metric's definition between runs â€” and no one is watching at 6am. Grounding routes every query through `semantic-mcp` over a governed registry, so columns are provably real, definitions are stable, and the same `revenue` means the same thing every run. The tradeoff is honest: I gave up the model's flexibility to express arbitrary novel queries, and I pay upfront cost building the semantic layer. But that's exactly the trade that buys a hard-zero hallucination gate, multi-tenant scale (add a registry, not a fork), and reproducibility. The thesis: the hard part of an AI analyst isn't generating insights, it's making them correct and trusted â€” and you can't trust SQL you can't prove.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** `semantic-mcp` as the sole SQL path over `semantic_layer.yaml` is grounding pillar #1; it's what makes "0 hallucinated columns" a CI gate rather than a hope.
**Follow-ups.** What's the largest class of legitimate query your grounded approach *can't* serve? / How do you keep the registry from becoming a bottleneck on analyst velocity? / Where would you allow generated SQL, if anywhere?

#### Q46. "Determinism where it matters" â€” where do you allow non-determinism, and where do you forbid it?
**Ideal answer.** The rule is: non-determinism is fine for *search*, forbidden for *truth*. I let the model be non-deterministic in which hypotheses it explores â€” that's creative search over a combinatorial space and variance there is acceptable because the Critic and significance gates filter it. I forbid non-determinism in three places: the math (all decomposition/significance/forecast/power run in seeded Python in `stats-mcp`, reproducible byte-for-byte), the SQL (emitted from a fixed registry, so two runs reference the same columns), and the control flow (a deterministic FSM, not the model, decides node order). So the model is a *composer inside a deterministic shell*: its variance is confined to hypothesis selection, and every number in the brief is a verbatim tool output reconstructable from `audit_log`. The failure I'm avoiding is "the AI said so" â€” a system where you can't tell whether a different answer means new data or just a different sample from the model.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Deterministic FSM + seeded `stats-mcp` + governed `semantic-mcp` confine non-determinism to which hypotheses Diagnose explores, which the Critic then filters.
**Follow-ups.** How do you make scipy/statsmodels/prophet runs actually reproducible? / If hypothesis selection varies, can two runs disagree on the top finding â€” and is that acceptable? / Where would more determinism hurt rather than help?

#### Q47. Build vs buy: you used Claude Agent SDK and MCP but built your own semantic layer, stats, and eval. Why that line?
**Ideal answer.** I bought the commodity and built the moat. The agent runtime, tool-calling protocol (MCP), and CI are commodities â€” well-solved, not differentiating â€” so I used the Claude Agent SDK and MCP rather than reinventing an orchestration framework. But the things that make Helios *trustworthy* are the differentiators, and those I built: the governed semantic layer (mapping 1:1 to dbt MetricFlow, so I'm not even reinventing metric definitions â€” I'm aligning with the standard), the deterministic stats in real Python, and the offline labeled eval. I also deliberately *didn't* buy a generic text-to-SQL or BI tool because their "validate after generate" model can't give me a hard-zero hallucination guarantee. The principle: build where correctness and trust live, buy where the problem is already solved, and don't build a framework when a protocol exists.
**Why Helios demonstrates it.** Stack = bought MCP + Claude Agent SDK + GitHub Actions; built `semantic_layer.yaml` (1:1 to MetricFlow), `stats-mcp`, and the `helios.eval` harness â€” the three trust pillars.
**Follow-ups.** Why MCP over a hand-rolled tool interface? / Would you have used a managed semantic layer (LookML, MetricFlow) if one fit? / What would you buy next as the project grows?

#### Q48. Cost control: how do you keep an autonomous, multi-agent, LLM-plus-BigQuery system from getting expensive?
